The Method of Reasoning by Established Names and the Two Types of Naming
Al-Nasafi introduces the method and the first classification of naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method of proving divine attributes through established names and the initial classification of naming into two types: those indicating absolute existence and those indicating a meaning beyond existence.

Core Idea
Divine names are either labels for existence or indicators of deeper meanings.

Al-Nasafi begins by presenting a key method for the people of truth: reasoning from the established names found in clear texts and consensus. He then immediately introduces a fundamental classification: naming either points to mere existence or to something more. This lesson unpacks that starting point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Pay attention to the terms 'established name' (al-ism al-thabit) and 'absolute existence' (mutlaq al-wujud).
2
Identify the two categories of naming
Find the two types of naming that al-Nasafi distinguishes: one for absolute existence and one for meaning beyond existence.
These categories form the foundation for the rest of the argument.
3
Understand the significance of 'established name'
Reflect on why al-Nasafi emphasizes that the name must be established by unambiguous texts and consensus.
This ensures the names we reason from are authoritative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لأهل الحق في المسألة طرق منها: طريقة الاستدلال بالاسم الثابت بالنصوص التي لا ريب في ثبوتها، والإجماع الذي لا مخالفة فيه في الأمة وهذه الطريقة أن يقال: إن الله تعالى يسمى حيا قادرا عالما سميعا بصيرا باقيا . ثم التسمية إما أن كانت وضعت لتدل على مطلق الوجود كلفظة الموجود والشيء . وإما أن كانت وضعت لتدل على معنى وراء الوجود. وذلك المعنى إما مائية يمتاز بها النوع من النوع كاسم الفرس والبغل والحمار والآدمي والأسد . فإن لكل مسمى من هذه الأشياء مائية يمتاز بها عن الآخر، من صورة مخصوصة، أو خاصية لازمة، وقد تكون تلك المائية والخاصية منقسمة إلى قسمين: أحدهما يرجع إلى معنى زائد على الذات، والثاني: يرجع على الخصوص والعموم. فتدل التسمية للقسم الأول أن له معنى وراء مطلق الوجود اختص به فصار لأجله نوعا على حدة كالنطق والاعتداء للآدمي. والقسم الثاني أنه خاص لا موجود مطلق، وإما صفة قائمة بالمسمى اشتق منها الاسم كالمتكلم والمريد، والأسود والأبيض، والمتحرك والساكن، فإن كل تسمية منها اشتقت من معنى يعرف عند إطلاق هذا الاسم ثبوت تلك الصفة. ثم إن هذه التسمية على ما عليه وضع الاشتقاق ثابتا، وعند انعدامه يعد كذبا ، إلا إذا نقل عن حقيقته وجعل لقبا للذات أو عَلَمًا له يمتاز به الذات عن أغياره من بين جنسه، فيكون موضوعا للذات لا باعتبار المعنى بل جعل عَلَمًا له من غير التعرض للمعنى، كما يلقب الأعمى بالبصير، ويمسى العبوس ضحاكا، والضحاك عباسا، ولهذا قال أهل اللغة: إن الأعلام والألقاب لا يقعان على الحقائق، والفرق بين كون اللفظ علما غير واقع على الحقائق وبين كونه وصفا للذات واقعا على الحقائق : أن العلم لا يثبت إلا لضرب اصطلاح، ولا يعرفه اسما للذات من لا وقوف له على ذلك الاصطلاح المعني، وإن لم يعرف الاصطلاح، ولا سمع أحدا أطلق عليه هذا الوصف.
Then we say: For the people of truth regarding the issue there are methods, among them: the method of reasoning by the established name (al-ism al-thabit) from the texts whose establishment is beyond doubt, and from the consensus in which there is no disagreement in the community. This method is that it is said: Allah, exalted, is called Living, Powerful, Knowing, Hearing, Seeing, Eternal. Then the naming (al-tasmiya) is either placed to indicate absolute existence (mutlaq al-wujud) like the word 'existent' (al-mawjud) and 'thing' (al-shay'), or it is placed to indicate a meaning beyond existence. That meaning is either a quiddity (mahiyya) by which a species is distinguished from another species, like the names 'horse', 'mule', 'donkey', 'human', and 'lion'. For each named object among these things has a quiddity by which it is distinguished from another, by a specific form or a necessary property. And that quiddity and property may be divided into two divisions: one goes back to a meaning additional to the essence (dhat), and the second goes back to specificity and generality. So the naming for the first division indicates that it has a meaning beyond absolute existence, by which it is distinguished, so it becomes for that reason a separate type, like speech and uprightness for humans. The second division: that it is specific, not absolutely existent, and rather it is an attribute (sifa) subsisting in the named object from which the name is derived, like 'speaker' and 'willer', 'black' and 'white', 'moving' and 'still'. For each of these names is derived from a meaning which, when this name is uttered, is known to affirm that attribute. Then this naming, according to the rule of derivation (wad' al-ishtiqaq), is established, and when it is absent it is considered false, unless it is transferred from its reality and made a nickname (laqab) for the essence or a proper name ('alam) for it by which the essence is distinguished from others of its kind, so it becomes placed for the essence not by consideration of meaning but made a proper name for it without addressing the meaning, just as a blind man is nicknamed 'the seeing one', and the frowning one is called 'the laughing one', and the laughing one is called 'the frowning one'. For this reason the linguists said: Proper names and nicknames do not apply to [indicate] realities. The difference between a word being a proper name not applying to a reality and being a description (wasf) of the essence applying to a reality is that the proper name is not established except by a convention (istilah), and no one knows it as a name for the essence except one who is aware of that intended convention, even if he does not know the convention [itself], nor has he heard anyone apply this description to it.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage introduces a methodological approach for proving the attributes of God by analyzing the divine names. Al-Nasafi states that among the methods for the people of truth is reasoning by the 'established name' (al-ism al-thabit) based on unambiguous texts and consensus. He begins by noting that Allah is called by names like Living, Powerful, Knowing, etc. He then classifies naming (tasmiya) into two types: names that indicate mere existence (like 'existent' and 'thing') and names that indicate a meaning beyond existence. Those that indicate a meaning beyond existence are further divided into two categories: the first are names that indicate a quiddity (mahiyya) that distinguishes a species, such as 'human' or 'horse'. This quiddity can be further divided into two subcategories: one where the meaning is additional to the essence (like speech for humans), and another where it indicates a specific attribute (like 'speaker' or 'white'). The second main category consists of names derived from attributes inherent to the named object. Al-Nasafi then discusses the rule of derivation: names derived from attributes are true only if the attribute is present; otherwise, they are false unless the name has been transferred from its literal meaning and used as a nickname or proper name. He gives examples: calling a blind man 'the seer' or a frowning man 'the laugher' as nicknames. He distinguishes between a proper name (which does not indicate a reality) and a descriptive title (which does). The key difference is that a proper name only works by convention and is not understood without knowledge of that convention, whereas a descriptive title directly indicates the attribute. This entire discussion sets the stage for proving that the divine names (like 'Living', 'Knowing') must be descriptive titles that indicate real attributes in God, since they are not mere proper names.

Key Terms
اسم ثابت
تسمية
وجود مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all divine names are simply proper names without inherent meaning.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that many divine names are derived from attributes and indicate real meanings beyond mere labels.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, what are the two types of naming?
A) Names that indicate existence and names that indicate non-existence
B) Names that indicate absolute existence and names that indicate a meaning beyond existence
C) Names derived from attributes and names derived from actions
D) Names that are proper and names that are descriptive

Key Takeaway
The method of reasoning by established names begins with a basic distinction: some names denote mere existence, while others denote real meanings beyond it.

This lesson calls you to see that names like 'Al-Knowing' imply a real attribute. Does your heart connect with that meaning, or do you recite them as empty labels?
?
This method uses definitive texts and consensus as its foundation. Have you built your understanding of Allah's attributes on such firm ground, or have you relied on hearsay? What concrete step will you take this week to engage directly with the Qur'anic verses about Allah's names?
?
The distinction between names that indicate mere existence and those that indicate a meaning beyond existence is crucial. In your daily life, when you say 'Allah is Alive,' do you feel the weight of that attribute, or is it just a word? The Categories of Meaning Beyond Existence: Quiddity and Attribute
Al-Nasafi classifies names indicating meaning beyond existence and discusses derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi further classifies names that indicate a meaning beyond existence into quiddity-distinguishing names and attribute-derived names, and the rule of derivation.

Core Idea
Names beyond mere existence refer either to what makes something unique or to an attribute it possesses.

Having established that some names point to meanings beyond existence, al-Nasafi now dives into a deeper classification. He distinguishes between names that indicate a quiddity (essence) that distinguishes a species and names that are derived from an attribute. He then discusses the crucial rule: such derived names are true only if the attribute is present, unless they have become mere nicknames or proper names.

Guided Path
1
Divide the second type: quiddity vs. attribute
Identify the two main categories under 'meaning beyond existence': quiddity (species distinction) and attribute (derived from a quality).
Examples: quiddity names like 'human'; attribute names like 'speaker'.
2
Understand the two subcategories of quiddity
Notice that quiddity can be divided into a meaning additional to the essence (e.g., speech for humans) and a specific/general property.
This shows that quiddities are not all the same.
3
Learn the rule of derivation (ishtiqaq)
Al-Nasafi states that a derived name is true only if the attribute is present; if absent, the name is false unless transferred to a nickname or proper name.
This is crucial for understanding how divine names work.
4
Distinguish proper names from descriptive titles
Compare proper names (convention-based) with descriptive titles (reality-based) to see why divine names must be descriptive.
Think of nicknames like 'seer' for a blind person—they don't indicate reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : لأهل الحق في المسألة طرق منها: طريقة الاستدلال بالاسم الثابت بالنصوص التي لا ريب في ثبوتها، والإجماع الذي لا مخالفة فيه في الأمة وهذه الطريقة أن يقال: إن الله تعالى يسمى حيا قادرا عالما سميعا بصيرا باقيا . ثم التسمية إما أن كانت وضعت لتدل على مطلق الوجود كلفظة الموجود والشيء . وإما أن كانت وضعت لتدل على معنى وراء الوجود. وذلك المعنى إما مائية يمتاز بها النوع من النوع كاسم الفرس والبغل والحمار والآدمي والأسد . فإن لكل مسمى من هذه الأشياء مائية يمتاز بها عن الآخر، من صورة مخصوصة، أو خاصية لازمة، وقد تكون تلك المائية والخاصية منقسمة إلى قسمين: أحدهما يرجع إلى معنى زائد على الذات، والثاني: يرجع على الخصوص والعموم. فتدل التسمية للقسم الأول أن له معنى وراء مطلق الوجود اختص به فصار لأجله نوعا على حدة كالنطق والاعتداء للآدمي. والقسم الثاني أنه خاص لا موجود مطلق، وإما صفة قائمة بالمسمى اشتق منها الاسم كالمتكلم والمريد، والأسود والأبيض، والمتحرك والساكن، فإن كل تسمية منها اشتقت من معنى يعرف عند إطلاق هذا الاسم ثبوت تلك الصفة. ثم إن هذه التسمية على ما عليه وضع الاشتقاق ثابتا، وعند انعدامه يعد كذبا ، إلا إذا نقل عن حقيقته وجعل لقبا للذات أو عَلَمًا له يمتاز به الذات عن أغياره من بين جنسه، فيكون موضوعا للذات لا باعتبار المعنى بل جعل عَلَمًا له من غير التعرض للمعنى، كما يلقب الأعمى بالبصير، ويمسى العبوس ضحاكا، والضحاك عباسا، ولهذا قال أهل اللغة: إن الأعلام والألقاب لا يقعان على الحقائق، والفرق بين كون اللفظ علما غير واقع على الحقائق وبين كونه وصفا للذات واقعا على الحقائق : أن العلم لا يثبت إلا لضرب اصطلاح، ولا يعرفه اسما للذات من لا وقوف له على ذلك الاصطلاح المعني، وإن لم يعرف الاصطلاح، ولا سمع أحدا أطلق عليه هذا الوصف.
Then we say: For the people of truth regarding the issue there are methods, among them: the method of reasoning by the established name (al-ism al-thabit) from the texts whose establishment is beyond doubt, and from the consensus in which there is no disagreement in the community. This method is that it is said: Allah, exalted, is called Living, Powerful, Knowing, Hearing, Seeing, Eternal. Then the naming (al-tasmiya) is either placed to indicate absolute existence (mutlaq al-wujud) like the word 'existent' (al-mawjud) and 'thing' (al-shay'), or it is placed to indicate a meaning beyond existence. That meaning is either a quiddity (mahiyya) by which a species is distinguished from another species, like the names 'horse', 'mule', 'donkey', 'human', and 'lion'. For each named object among these things has a quiddity by which it is distinguished from another, by a specific form or a necessary property. And that quiddity and property may be divided into two divisions: one goes back to a meaning additional to the essence (dhat), and the second goes back to specificity and generality. So the naming for the first division indicates that it has a meaning beyond absolute existence, by which it is distinguished, so it becomes for that reason a separate type, like speech and uprightness for humans. The second division: that it is specific, not absolutely existent, and rather it is an attribute (sifa) subsisting in the named object from which the name is derived, like 'speaker' and 'willer', 'black' and 'white', 'moving' and 'still'. For each of these names is derived from a meaning which, when this name is uttered, is known to affirm that attribute. Then this naming, according to the rule of derivation (wad' al-ishtiqaq), is established, and when it is absent it is considered false, unless it is transferred from its reality and made a nickname (laqab) for the essence or a proper name ('alam) for it by which the essence is distinguished from others of its kind, so it becomes placed for the essence not by consideration of meaning but made a proper name for it without addressing the meaning, just as a blind man is nicknamed 'the seeing one', and the frowning one is called 'the laughing one', and the laughing one is called 'the frowning one'. For this reason the linguists said: Proper names and nicknames do not apply to [indicate] realities. The difference between a word being a proper name not applying to a reality and being a description (wasf) of the essence applying to a reality is that the proper name is not established except by a convention (istilah), and no one knows it as a name for the essence except one who is aware of that intended convention, even if he does not know the convention [itself], nor has he heard anyone apply this description to it.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage introduces a methodological approach for proving the attributes of God by analyzing the divine names. Al-Nasafi states that among the methods for the people of truth is reasoning by the 'established name' (al-ism al-thabit) based on unambiguous texts and consensus. He begins by noting that Allah is called by names like Living, Powerful, Knowing, etc. He then classifies naming (tasmiya) into two types: names that indicate mere existence (like 'existent' and 'thing') and names that indicate a meaning beyond existence. Those that indicate a meaning beyond existence are further divided into two categories: the first are names that indicate a quiddity (mahiyya) that distinguishes a species, such as 'human' or 'horse'. This quiddity can be further divided into two subcategories: one where the meaning is additional to the essence (like speech for humans), and another where it indicates a specific attribute (like 'speaker' or 'white'). The second main category consists of names derived from attributes inherent to the named object. Al-Nasafi then discusses the rule of derivation: names derived from attributes are true only if the attribute is present; otherwise, they are false unless the name has been transferred from its literal meaning and used as a nickname or proper name. He gives examples: calling a blind man 'the seer' or a frowning man 'the laugher' as nicknames. He distinguishes between a proper name (which does not indicate a reality) and a descriptive title (which does). The key difference is that a proper name only works by convention and is not understood without knowledge of that convention, whereas a descriptive title directly indicates the attribute. This entire discussion sets the stage for proving that the divine names (like 'Living', 'Knowing') must be descriptive titles that indicate real attributes in God, since they are not mere proper names.

Key Terms
مائية
خاصية
صفة
اشتقاق
لقب
علم
وصف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that calling someone a 'seer' when blind is a lie in all cases.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explains that if it's a nickname (laqab) or proper name, it's not lying because it's not intended to indicate the attribute.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, when is a derived name (like 'speaker') considered false?
A) When the person is not actually speaking
B) When it is used as a nickname
C) When it indicates a quiddity
D) When it is derived from an action

Key Takeaway
Names that go beyond mere existence either capture what makes something unique or derive from a real attribute, and such derived names are truthful only if the attribute is present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does it affect your reliance on the names of Allah that they are not mere labels but reflect real attributes? Do you call upon Allah by His names with the conviction that He truly possesses those attributes? Examine your du'a: do you ask Allah by His names as if they are empty titles or as if they truly mean He is all-knowing, all-powerful? What change must you make today to ensure your heart connects the name to the reality?
?
When you describe yourself with derived names like 'believer' or 'patient', are you aware that the truth of that description depends on the attribute being present in you? How often do you take such names lightly? Ask yourself: do I truly possess the attribute of patience, or is my claim empty? If empty, what must I do to bring reality to the name?
?
The scholars distinguish between a nickname and a descriptive title. Are you guilty of treating some of Allah's names as mere nicknames—familiar terms without awe? Is your heart engaged when you say 'Ya Rahman'? If not, what is the barrier, and what step will you take today to break it?

Divine Attributes

Divine Names Are Derived from Meanings
Al-Nasafi presents the derived nature of divine names and the proof from distinct benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine names must imply real attributes and cannot be mere labels.

Core Idea
Divine names are not empty titles; each affirms a distinct meaning.

Al-Nasafi begins by establishing that the divine names are derived words (mushtaqqa). When applied to God, they must affirm the meaning from which they are taken, not just the essence. He argues that treating them as mere labels would reduce praise to mockery and lead to absurd repetition. This lesson presents his core reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the premise
Understand that these names are derived from meanings. They are not arbitrary labels.
Derivation means the word itself points to a concept.
2
Follow the reductio
If they were mere titles, applying laudatory words without meaning would be mockery, like calling a blind person 'seeing'.
3
Analyze the benefit argument
If each name only affirmed the essence, then 'living essence' and 'powerful essence' would just repeat 'essence'. But each gives a distinct benefit, so they must affirm different meanings.
This is the key proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ريب أن هذه الألفاظ من قبيل الألفاظ المشتقة عن المعاني، فإذا أطلقت على الذات يراد بها إثبات ما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق، لا إثبات الذات فحسب كما في اسم المتكلم والمتحرك والساكن وغيرها. ولو أطلقت هذه الألفاظ ولم يرد بها إثبات المعاني لكانت ألقابا أو أعلاما وجعل ما هو من صفات المدح لقبا لذات ما، وإطلاقه عليه عند عدم المعنى الذي مأخذ الاشتقاق نوع من الهزء والسخرية، كالأعمى يسمى بصيرا، والزنجي يسمى أبيض. ومن جعل إطلاق الأنبياء والمرسلين وعباد الله الصالحين، الأسماء الحسنى والصفات العلى على الخالق على طريق الهزء والسخرية فهو غير عارف بالله تعالى وبرسله عليهم السلام، وكذا كان الله تعالى بأمره عباده بالثناء عليه بهذه الأسماء أمرا بالسخرية وكفر من جوز هذا ما لا يخفي. يحققه أن هذه الأسامي لو كانت غير مثبتة للمعاني وكانت ألقابا وأعلاما لم يثبت بكل لفظ منها إلا الذات فيثبت بقولنا: حي الذات، وكذا بقولنا : قادر وعالم، فيصير قولنا: إن الله تعالى ذات عالم قادر حي سميع يصير قولا بأنه تعالى ذات ذات ذات ذات ذات ذات ولم يحصل بكل لفظ فائدة سوى ما حصله باللفظة الأولى، وحيث لم يكن كذلك بل ما حصل بكل لفظ للسامع من الفائدة ما لا يحصل بغيره من الألفاظ، علم أن هذه الأسامي ما أطلقت إلا لإثبات ما فيها من المعاني. واعتراض بعض أغبيائهم على هذا ــ بأن حصول فائدة بكل لفظ لا يدل على ثبوت معنى وراء الذات. فإن بقولنا: عرض يحصل من الفائدة ما لا يحصل بقولنا: موجود، وبقولنا: كون، يحصل ما لا يحصل بقولنا : لون، وعلى ذلك كل لفظ من هذه الألفاظ لا يدل على معنى وراء الذات اعتراض صدر عن الجهل بالإلزام وبيان ذلك أنا بينا أن هذه الأسامي مشتقة، لو أطلقت ولم يرد بها ذلك لكانت ألقابا وأعلاما متناوله للذات، لا لما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق من المعاني فيصير كتكرير الذات وليس كذلك لفهم السامع بل لكل لفظة ما لا يفهم بما سواها من الألفاظ، فدل أنه ما أريد بها اللقب والعلم، إما أريد بها ما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق بكل لفظة . ولم نقل إن حصول فائدة على حدة بكل لفظ يدل على ثبوت معنى وراء الذات في جميع الأسامي، بل ندعي أن فيما نحن فيه لم ننقل هذه الأسامي المشتقة عن المعاني عن حقائقها إلى إرادة مجرد الذات بها بطريق اللقب، والعلم لبقائها دالة على المعاني بدليل حصول الفائدة، فإذا فرضنا من هذا بيان إثبات هذه الأسامي على حقائقها، وهي بحقائقها توجب ثبوت المعاني، فأما فيما وراء ذلك من الأسامي الجارية مجرى الخصوم
There is no doubt that these words are of the type of words derived from meanings. So when they are applied to the Essence, what is intended by them is the affirmation of that from which the derivation is taken, not merely the affirmation of the Essence, as in the names 'speaker', 'moving', 'stationary', and others. And if these words were applied without intending the affirmation of the meanings, they would be titles or proper names, and making what is from the attributes of praise a title for some essence, and applying it to Him in the absence of the meaning from which the derivation is taken, is a kind of mockery and ridicule—like calling a blind person 'seeing', and a black person 'white'. And whoever makes the application by the prophets, messengers, and righteous servants of God of the most beautiful names and the highest attributes to the Creator by way of mockery and ridicule is not a knower of God Most High and His messengers, peace be upon them. Likewise, if God Most High had commanded His servants to praise Him with these names, it would be a command to mockery, and disbelief of whoever permits this is not hidden. He verifies it by saying: If these names were not affirming the meanings and were titles and proper names, then from each word only the Essence would be affirmed. So by our saying 'living Essence', and likewise by our saying 'powerful' and 'knowing', our saying 'God Most High is a knowing, powerful, living, hearing Essence' would become a saying that He is an Essence, Essence, Essence, Essence, Essence, and no benefit would be obtained from each word except what was obtained from the first word. And since it is not so, but rather from each word the listener obtains a benefit that is not obtained from other words, it is known that these names are only applied to affirm the meanings in them. And the objection by some of their ignoramuses to this—that obtaining benefit from each word does not indicate the existence of a meaning beyond the Essence, because by our saying 'substance' a benefit is obtained that is not obtained by our saying 'existent', and by our saying 'being' a benefit is obtained that is not obtained by our saying 'color', and so on, so each of these words does not indicate a meaning beyond the Essence—is an objection arising from ignorance of the implication. The explanation of that is that we have shown that these names are derived; if they were applied without intending that, they would be titles and proper names pertaining to the Essence, not to that from which the derivation is taken of the meanings, and so it would become like repetition of the Essence. But it is not so according to the listener's understanding; rather, for each word there is something that is not understood from other words. So it indicates that the title and proper name are not intended by them; rather, what is intended by each word is that from which the derivation is taken. And we did not say that obtaining a separate benefit from each word indicates the existence of a meaning beyond the Essence in all names. Rather, we claim that in the case we are discussing, we do not transfer these names derived from meanings from their true meanings to intending merely the Essence by way of title and proper name, because they remain indicative of the meanings by the evidence of obtaining benefit. So if we suppose from this the explanation of affirming these names upon their true meanings, and they in their true meanings necessitate the existence of the meanings. As for beyond that of the names that run the course of [؟]
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine names are derived and indicate real attributes. The author begins by stating the premise that these names are derived from meanings (mashqūqah ʿan al-maʿānī). When applied to God's essence, they are intended to affirm the meaning from which they are derived, not just the essence itself. He then argues by reductio: if they were mere titles (alqāb) or proper names (aʿlām), applying laudatory attributes without corresponding meaning would be mockery, like calling a blind person 'seeing'. Moreover, if each name only affirmed the essence, then saying 'knowing essence, powerful essence' would be mere repetition of 'essence', yielding no new information. But in fact each name conveys a distinct benefit to the listener, so it must be that each intends the specific meaning. He then addresses an objection: the opponent claims that distinct benefit does not prove a meaning beyond the essence, citing examples like 'substance' and 'existent' which refer to the same thing but give different benefits. The author responds by clarifying that his argument is specific: these derived names, if taken as mere labels, would not provide distinct benefits, but they do, so they must retain their derived meanings. He further clarifies that he does not claim this for all names universally, but only for these derived names in this context. Thus, each derived name necessarily implies a real attribute in the essence.

ذات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine names are just empty titles that do not denote real attributes.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that if they were empty titles, they would lead to absurd repetition and imply mockery. The distinct benefit each name provides proves they affirm real meanings.

D) They would indicate God's actions, not His essence.

Key Takeaway
Divine names are derived words that necessarily affirm real attributes in God, as proven by the distinct benefit each name conveys.

Refuting the Objection: Distinct Benefit Does Not Always Imply a Meaning Beyond Essence
Al-Nasafi responds to the objection that distinct benefit does not imply meaning beyond essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection based on 'substance' and 'existent' fails, and how al-Nasafi limits his argument to derived names.

Core Idea
Not every word with distinct benefit implies a separate attribute; only derived names retain their meanings.

Having presented his proof, al-Nasafi addresses an opponent who claims that distinct benefit does not prove meaning beyond essence, citing examples like 'substance' and 'existent'. Al-Nasafi clarifies that his argument is specifically about derived names in the divine context, not all words universally. This lesson unpacks his rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The opponent says: 'substance' and 'existent' give different benefits but both refer to the same thing, without implying additional meanings.
This is a challenge to the benefit argument.
2
Identify al-Nasafi's response
He explains that his claim is not universal; it applies only to these derived names. He shows that derived names, if taken as mere labels, would not provide distinct benefits, but they do, so they must retain their derived meanings.
3
Understand the limitation
Al-Nasafi does not claim that every word with distinct benefit indicates a meaning beyond the referent. Rather, in the case of divine names, because they are derived and known to be so, they must affirm the specific meaning.
This preserves the argument from the counterexample.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا ريب أن هذه الألفاظ من قبيل الألفاظ المشتقة عن المعاني، فإذا أطلقت على الذات يراد بها إثبات ما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق، لا إثبات الذات فحسب كما في اسم المتكلم والمتحرك والساكن وغيرها. ولو أطلقت هذه الألفاظ ولم يرد بها إثبات المعاني لكانت ألقابا أو أعلاما وجعل ما هو من صفات المدح لقبا لذات ما، وإطلاقه عليه عند عدم المعنى الذي مأخذ الاشتقاق نوع من الهزء والسخرية، كالأعمى يسمى بصيرا، والزنجي يسمى أبيض. ومن جعل إطلاق الأنبياء والمرسلين وعباد الله الصالحين، الأسماء الحسنى والصفات العلى على الخالق على طريق الهزء والسخرية فهو غير عارف بالله تعالى وبرسله عليهم السلام، وكذا كان الله تعالى بأمره عباده بالثناء عليه بهذه الأسماء أمرا بالسخرية وكفر من جوز هذا ما لا يخفي. يحققه أن هذه الأسامي لو كانت غير مثبتة للمعاني وكانت ألقابا وأعلاما لم يثبت بكل لفظ منها إلا الذات فيثبت بقولنا: حي الذات، وكذا بقولنا : قادر وعالم، فيصير قولنا: إن الله تعالى ذات عالم قادر حي سميع يصير قولا بأنه تعالى ذات ذات ذات ذات ذات ذات ولم يحصل بكل لفظ فائدة سوى ما حصله باللفظة الأولى، وحيث لم يكن كذلك بل ما حصل بكل لفظ للسامع من الفائدة ما لا يحصل بغيره من الألفاظ، علم أن هذه الأسامي ما أطلقت إلا لإثبات ما فيها من المعاني. واعتراض بعض أغبيائهم على هذا ــ بأن حصول فائدة بكل لفظ لا يدل على ثبوت معنى وراء الذات. فإن بقولنا: عرض يحصل من الفائدة ما لا يحصل بقولنا: موجود، وبقولنا: كون، يحصل ما لا يحصل بقولنا : لون، وعلى ذلك كل لفظ من هذه الألفاظ لا يدل على معنى وراء الذات اعتراض صدر عن الجهل بالإلزام وبيان ذلك أنا بينا أن هذه الأسامي مشتقة، لو أطلقت ولم يرد بها ذلك لكانت ألقابا وأعلاما متناوله للذات، لا لما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق من المعاني فيصير كتكرير الذات وليس كذلك لفهم السامع بل لكل لفظة ما لا يفهم بما سواها من الألفاظ، فدل أنه ما أريد بها اللقب والعلم، إما أريد بها ما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق بكل لفظة . ولم نقل إن حصول فائدة على حدة بكل لفظ يدل على ثبوت معنى وراء الذات في جميع الأسامي، بل ندعي أن فيما نحن فيه لم ننقل هذه الأسامي المشتقة عن المعاني عن حقائقها إلى إرادة مجرد الذات بها بطريق اللقب، والعلم لبقائها دالة على المعاني بدليل حصول الفائدة، فإذا فرضنا من هذا بيان إثبات هذه الأسامي على حقائقها، وهي بحقائقها توجب ثبوت المعاني، فأما فيما وراء ذلك من الأسامي الجارية مجرى الخصوم
There is no doubt that these words are of the type of words derived from meanings. So when they are applied to the Essence, what is intended by them is the affirmation of that from which the derivation is taken, not merely the affirmation of the Essence, as in the names 'speaker', 'moving', 'stationary', and others. And if these words were applied without intending the affirmation of the meanings, they would be titles or proper names, and making what is from the attributes of praise a title for some essence, and applying it to Him in the absence of the meaning from which the derivation is taken, is a kind of mockery and ridicule—like calling a blind person 'seeing', and a black person 'white'. And whoever makes the application by the prophets, messengers, and righteous servants of God of the most beautiful names and the highest attributes to the Creator by way of mockery and ridicule is not a knower of God Most High and His messengers, peace be upon them. Likewise, if God Most High had commanded His servants to praise Him with these names, it would be a command to mockery, and disbelief of whoever permits this is not hidden. He verifies it by saying: If these names were not affirming the meanings and were titles and proper names, then from each word only the Essence would be affirmed. So by our saying 'living Essence', and likewise by our saying 'powerful' and 'knowing', our saying 'God Most High is a knowing, powerful, living, hearing Essence' would become a saying that He is an Essence, Essence, Essence, Essence, Essence, and no benefit would be obtained from each word except what was obtained from the first word. And since it is not so, but rather from each word the listener obtains a benefit that is not obtained from other words, it is known that these names are only applied to affirm the meanings in them. And the objection by some of their ignoramuses to this—that obtaining benefit from each word does not indicate the existence of a meaning beyond the Essence, because by our saying 'substance' a benefit is obtained that is not obtained by our saying 'existent', and by our saying 'being' a benefit is obtained that is not obtained by our saying 'color', and so on, so each of these words does not indicate a meaning beyond the Essence—is an objection arising from ignorance of the implication. The explanation of that is that we have shown that these names are derived; if they were applied without intending that, they would be titles and proper names pertaining to the Essence, not to that from which the derivation is taken of the meanings, and so it would become like repetition of the Essence. But it is not so according to the listener's understanding; rather, for each word there is something that is not understood from other words. So it indicates that the title and proper name are not intended by them; rather, what is intended by each word is that from which the derivation is taken. And we did not say that obtaining a separate benefit from each word indicates the existence of a meaning beyond the Essence in all names. Rather, we claim that in the case we are discussing, we do not transfer these names derived from meanings from their true meanings to intending merely the Essence by way of title and proper name, because they remain indicative of the meanings by the evidence of obtaining benefit. So if we suppose from this the explanation of affirming these names upon their true meanings, and they in their true meanings necessitate the existence of the meanings. As for beyond that of the names that run the course of [؟]
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a proof that divine names are derived and indicate real attributes. The author begins by stating the premise that these names are derived from meanings (mashqūqah ʿan al-maʿānī). When applied to God's essence, they are intended to affirm the meaning from which they are derived, not just the essence itself. He then argues by reductio: if they were mere titles (alqāb) or proper names (aʿlām), applying laudatory attributes without corresponding meaning would be mockery, like calling a blind person 'seeing'. Moreover, if each name only affirmed the essence, then saying 'knowing essence, powerful essence' would be mere repetition of 'essence', yielding no new information. But in fact each name conveys a distinct benefit to the listener, so it must be that each intends the specific meaning. He then addresses an objection: the opponent claims that distinct benefit does not prove a meaning beyond the essence, citing examples like 'substance' and 'existent' which refer to the same thing but give different benefits. The author responds by clarifying that his argument is specific: these derived names, if taken as mere labels, would not provide distinct benefits, but they do, so they must retain their derived meanings. He further clarifies that he does not claim this for all names universally, but only for these derived names in this context. Thus, each derived name necessarily implies a real attribute in the essence.

Key Terms
فائدة
اشتقاق
لقب
علم
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a word provides distinct information, it must always indicate a separate entity or attribute beyond the referent.
Correction: Al-Nasafi limits this to derived names: words like 'substance' and 'existent' give different benefits but refer to the same entity under different aspects, not to separate attributes.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi respond to the objection that 'substance' and 'existent' provide different benefits without indicating separate meanings?
A) He concedes the objection and modifies his argument.
B) He argues that 'substance' and 'existent' are also derived names.
C) He explains that his argument is specific to derived names, not all words.
D) He claims that 'substance' and 'existent' do indicate separate entities.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's proof for divine attributes is specific to derived names; it does not apply to all words that give distinct benefits, as the objection from 'substance' and 'existent' shows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the names of Allah (e.g., al-Rahman, al-Rahim), do you truly consider that each name conveys a distinct attribute that requires a separate meaning in the divine essence? Or do you take them as mere labels that could be interchanged? Let this lesson challenge your casual understanding.
?
Al-Nasafi criticizes the opponent for applying a general rule carelessly. In your own life, where have you mistakenly applied a general principle to a specific situation, causing misunderstanding? Are you willing to search your heart and correct that today?
?
The precision of al-Nasafi's argument requires attention to detail and willingness to accept that some names are derived but others are not. How does your own approach to studying divine names reflect this care? Are you content with surface-level knowledge, or do you strive for the depth that this lesson demands?

Al-Baghdadi's Classification of Divine Names
Al-Baghdadi categorizes divine names into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baghdadi classifies divine names into three categories: those indicating the essence, those indicating eternal attributes, and those indicating actions.

Core Idea
Divine names point to God's essence, attributes, or acts.

Al-Baghdadi offers a clear framework for understanding the different types of divine names. This framework helps avoid confusion between names that describe God's essence, His eternal attributes, and His actions. His classification is foundational for later discussions in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Focus on the examples and the categories.
The passage is from al-Baghdadi's Usul al-Din.
2
Identify the three categories
Look for the three categories: names indicating essence, names indicating eternal attributes, and names indicating actions. Note the examples given.
3
Understand the rationale
Notice how al-Baghdadi explains that names derived from a meaning subsisting in God point to an eternal attribute.
4
Reflect on the incomplete part
The passage ends with a fourth category that is cut off. Consider what it might be based on the pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي هذا يقول البغدادي: كل ما كان من أسمائه مشتقا من معنى قائم به فذلك المعنى صفة له أزلية كالحي ..والقادر. والسميع والبصير.. لأن هذه الأسماء دالة على حياته وقدرته وسمعه وبصره. من ذلك أيضا الودود والحليم والصبور من أسمائه راجع إلى معنى إرادته للإنعام على عبده أو إرادته للعفو عنه. وكل اسم كان مشتقا من صفة له أزلية فذلك الاسم من أسمائه الأزلية. ثم هناك أسماء تدل على أفعاله كالبر تدل على بره بعباده والباسط تدل على بسط الرزق لمن شاء والباعث من أسمائه دليل على بعثه الرسل وعلى بعثه الأموات من اللحود والثواب أنه الموفق عباده للتوبة ... والساتر والستار دليل على ستره ذنوب من يشاء من عباده وإلى ستره عيوب من شاء منهم وكذا الكفيل راجع إلى ضمانه أرزاق عباده. والنصير دليل على نصرته لأوليائه على أعدائه. راجع أصول الدين للبغدادي ص 122 – 126. ونخلص من هذا بأن هناك من أسمائه جل وعلا ما يدل على ذاته كشيء وموجود ومنها ما يدل على صفات أزلية كالقادر والعالم ومنها ما يدل على أفعاله كالخالق والباسط ومنها يدل على [؟]
And on this al-Baghdadi says: Every one of His names that is derived from a meaning subsisting in Him, that meaning is an eternal attribute for Him, like 'the Living', 'the Powerful', 'the Hearing', 'the Seeing'—because these names indicate His life, His power, His hearing, and His sight. Also from that are 'the Loving', 'the Forbearing', 'the Patient' among His names, referring to the meaning of His will to bestow favor on His servant or His will to forgive him. And every name that is derived from an eternal attribute of His, that name is among His eternal names. Then there are names that indicate His actions, like 'the Righteous' indicates His righteousness to His servants, and 'the Extender' indicates His extending provision to whom He wills, and 'the Sender' among His names is evidence of His sending the messengers and His sending the dead from the graves, and 'the Rewarder' that He is the one who grants success to His servants for repentance... And 'the Coverer' and 'the Veiler' indicate His covering of sins of whom He wills of His servants and His covering of faults of whom He wills among them, and likewise 'the Guarantor' refers to His guarantee of the provisions of His servants. And 'the Helper' indicates His help for His allies against His enemies. Refer to Usul al-Din by al-Baghdadi pp. 122–126. And we conclude from this that among His names, may He be exalted, are those that indicate His essence, like 'thing' and 'existent', and among them are those that indicate eternal attributes, like 'the Powerful' and 'the Knowing', and among them are those that indicate His actions, like 'the Creator' and 'the Extender', and among them are those that indicate [؟]
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine names from al-Baghdadi's Usul al-Din. It begins by stating that any name derived from a meaning (ma'nā) that subsists in God, such as 'Living', 'Powerful', indicates that meaning as an eternal attribute (ṣifa azaliyya). Examples like 'Loving' and 'Forbearing' refer to God's will. Names derived from eternal attributes are themselves eternal names. A second category includes names that indicate God's actions (afʿāl), such as 'Righteous' (al-Barr) indicating His righteousness, 'Extender' (al-Basit) indicating extending provision, 'Sender' (al-Baʿith) indicating sending messengers and resurrection, 'Coverer' (al-Sātir) indicating covering sins, and 'Helper' (al-Nasīr) indicating helping allies. The passage then summarizes that some names indicate the essence (e.g., 'thing', 'existent'), some indicate eternal attributes (e.g., 'Powerful', 'Knowing'), and some indicate actions (e.g., 'Creator', 'Extender'). The text is cut off before completing the fourth category.

أفعال (af'al)
أسماء (asma')
ذات (dhat)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine names are of the same type and indicate the same thing.
Correction: Al-Baghdadi shows that divine names are diverse: some denote the essence, others eternal attributes, and others actions.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Baghdadi's classification shows that divine names are not uniform but reflect different aspects of God: His essence, eternal attributes, and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How often do you think of Him as actively relating to you through His names? What would change today if you truly believed He is right now extending provision to you? Commit to one specific way you will respond to that action today.
?
Al-Baghdadi distinguishes between names indicating eternal attributes and those indicating actions. Which aspect of Allah do you tend to neglect: His eternal attributes (like His power) or His active involvement (like His covering of sins)? How does this imbalance affect your worship? Resolve to meditate on one action-name this week and let it shape your dua.

Refutation of the Objection Against Derived Divine Names
Nasafi refutes the objection that derived divine names do not imply attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that derived divine names are not mere labels for the essence but retain their original meanings, thereby implying distinct attributes.

Core Idea
Derived names are not empty labels; each adds a unique meaning beyond the essence.

Nasafi confronts an objection that claims names like 'Knowing' and 'Powerful' are just alternative titles for God's essence, not indicators of separate attributes. He argues that if they were mere labels, they would be repetitive and pointless. Instead, each name conveys a distinct understanding, proving they retain their derivational meanings. This passage is a masterclass in conceptual precision and the logic of divine names.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage to find the objection: that derived names do not indicate a meaning beyond the essence. Label this as the view being refuted.
The objection is presented as a claim that each word 'does not indicate a meaning beyond the essence'.
2
Understand the conditional argument
Nasafi argues: if these names were used as mere titles for the essence, they would be repetitive. He uses the phrase 'they would be titles and proper names... becoming a repetition of the essence'.
This shows that mere labels add no new information.
3
Grasp the key evidence
The listener's understanding differs for each word. Nasafi says 'each word gives what is not understood by other words', proving they are not mere labels.
This is the central proof that the names retain their derivational meanings.
4
Note the important distinction
Nasafi clarifies that this argument applies specifically to derived divine names, not to general terms like 'existent' which indicate the essence with a specification.
He avoids overgeneralizing his claim.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Based on this reasoning, the objection is shown to be corrupt. Meditate on how this preserves the reality of attributes.
The success of the argument hinges on the distinct benefit each name provides.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحصل ما لا يحصل بقولنا لون وعلى ذلك كل لفظ من هذه الألفاظ لا يدل على معنى وراء الذات اعتراض صدر عن الجهل بالإلزام وبيان ذلك أنا بينا أن هذه الأسامي مشتقة لو أطلقت ولم يرد بها ذلك لكانت ألقابا وأعلاما متناوله للذات لا لما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق من المعاني فيصير كتكرير الذات وليس كذلك لفهم السامع بل لكل لفظة ما لا يفهم بما سواها من الألفاظ فدل أنه ما أريد بها اللقب والعلم إما أريد بها ما هو مأخذ الاشتقاق بكل لفظة ولم نقل إن حصول فائدة على حدة بكل لفظ يدل على ثبوت معنى وراء الذات في جميع الأسامي بل ندعي أن فيما نحن فيه لم ننقل هذه الأسامي المشتقة عن المعاني عن حقائقها إلى إرادة مجرد الذات بها بطريق اللقب والعلم لبقائها دالة على المعاني بدليل حصول الفائدة فإذا فرضنا من هذا بيان إثبات هذه الأسامي على حقائقها وهي بحقائقها توجب ثبوت المعاني فأما فيما وراء ذلك من الأسامي الجارية مجرى الخصوم والعموم الموضوعة بعضها لمطلق الوجود وبعضها لموجود مخصوص فلا تدعي أن حصول الفائدة بكل لفظ يدل على ثبوت معنى وراء الذات بل نقول هناك زيادة الفائدة بكل لفظ لدلالته على ما وضع له كل لفظ من إثبات زيادة خصوص في الموجود وفيما نحن فيه زيادة الفائدة أيضا لكل لفظ لدلالته على ما وضع له كل لفظ من إثبات معنى وراء ما وضع له اللفظ الآخر لدلالته عليه وبالوقف على هذه الجملة يعرف فساد الاعتراض والله الموفق
What is obtained that is not obtained by our saying 'color', and accordingly every word of these words does not indicate a meaning beyond the essence—an objection arising from ignorance of the implication. The explanation of that is that we have shown that these names are derived. If they were used absolutely without intending that, they would be titles and proper names encompassing the essence, not [indicating] what is the basis of derivation from meanings, and thus they would become a repetition of the essence, but it is not so in the understanding of the listener. Rather, each word gives what is not understood by other words. So it indicates that they are not intended as titles and proper names; rather, they are intended with what is the basis of derivation in each word. And we do not say that the obtaining of a separate benefit by each word indicates the establishment of a meaning beyond the essence in all names; rather, we claim in this matter that we have not transferred these derived names [from their original meanings] from their realities to intending the mere essence by them by way of title and proper name, because they remain indicating the meanings, as evidenced by the obtaining of benefit. So if we suppose from this a clarification of establishing these names upon their realities, and they, by their realities, necessitate the establishment of meanings. As for beyond that among the names that proceed in the manner of general terms—some placed for absolute existence and some for a particular existent—do not claim that the obtaining of benefit by each word indicates the establishment of a meaning beyond the essence. Rather, we say there is an increase of benefit by each word because it indicates what each word is placed for, of establishing an increase of particularity in the existent. And in our case, there is also an increase of benefit for each word because it indicates what each word is placed for, of establishing a meaning beyond what the other word is placed for, because it indicates it. By pausing on this summary, the corruption of the objection is known. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objection claimed that derived divine names (e.g., 'Knowing') do not indicate a meaning (ma'na) beyond the essence (dhat). Nasafi begins by labeling this objection an 'objection arising from ignorance of the implication' (jahil bi'l-ilzam). He then explains his reasoning: He has previously shown that these names are derived (mushtaqqa). If they were used merely as titles or proper names (alqab wa a'lam) for the essence, they would be repetitive (takrir al-dhat) and would not convey distinct understandings. However, each word gives something different to the listener, proving they are not mere labels but retain their original derivational meanings. He clarifies that he does not claim that every name (e.g., general terms like 'existent') implies a meaning beyond the essence in all cases; some names indicate the essence itself or specify existence. But for the derived names under discussion, they have not been transferred from their real meanings to mere labels, as evidenced by the benefit (fa'ida) they convey: each adds a distinct understanding. He concludes that by reflecting on this argument, the corruption of the objection becomes clear.

اشتقاق (ishtiqāq)
مشتق (mushtaq)
لقب (laqab)
علم ('alam)
ذات (dhāt)
فائدة (fā'ida)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Derived divine names like 'Knowing' are just alternative titles for God's essence and do not indicate any real attributes.
Correction: Nasafi argues that if they were mere titles, they would be repetitive and convey no unique understanding; but each name provides a distinct benefit, proving they retain their original meanings and thus imply attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the main evidence that derived divine names are not mere labels for the essence?
A) They are used in the Qur'an.
B) Each word conveys a distinct understanding not obtained by other words.
C) The names are numerous.
D) They are derived from verbs.

Key Takeaway
Derived divine names are not mere labels; each conveys a unique meaning, necessitating the reality of distinct attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection says that names are just labels—do you ever find yourself thinking of Allah's attributes as abstract concepts disconnected from His essence? How does this affect your trust and love for Him?
?
If each name adds a distinct meaning, then invoking 'Al-ʿAlīm' (The All-Knowing) should fill you with awe of His knowledge. In what area of your life do you most need to remember that Allah is not just a name but truly Knowing and Powerful?

Nasafi's Proof That Divine Names Indicate Real Attributes
Nasafi proves divine names indicate real attributes through the impossibility of simple negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that divine names are not mere labels but signify real attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
Divine names are not just labels; they affirm real attributes without which statements become contradictory.

Nasafi addresses a central debate in kalam: Do God's names merely label His essence, or do they indicate distinct attributes? He presents a crisp logical argument based on what happens when we negate a name. Let's follow his reasoning and see why the names must be real.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, familiarizing yourself with the flow. Then read the English translation to grasp the argument.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if ... then ...'.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Nasafi's proof targets those who say divine names are merely proper names for the essence, not indicating additional attributes. Identify that claim in the passage.
The opponent's view is implied in the proof's structure.
3
Analyze the conditional
Notice the key conditional: If names were mere labels, then saying 'He is existent but not knowing' would not be contradictory. Why would that be? Reflect on the logic.
4
Consider the consequence
Nasafi argues that if names only affirmed the essence, negating them would negate the essence itself—an absurd result. This shows the names must indicate real attributes.
Think about the difference between negating an attribute and negating existence.
5
Draw the conclusion
Wrap up by stating the conclusion: divine names are established on reality and indicate real meanings, not just the essence.
This refutes the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن هذه الأسامي قررت على الحقيقة وكانت دالة على المعاني وما نقلت عن الوضع الأصلي إلى جعلها أعلاما دالة على الذات فحسب أن يبقى اسم من الأسامي لا يفهم نفي الذات ولم يصر القائل بقوله هو موجود وليس بعالم مناقضا كما يصير بقوله موجود وليس بموجود ولو كان الاسم اسما لإثبات الذات فحسب لا ينفي بنفيه ما يثبت بثبوته فينتفي الذات كما لو قيل ليس بموجود أو ليس بشيء وحيث الأمر على ما قررنا دل ذلك على بطلان كلامهم
And the proof that these names are established upon reality and are indicative of the meanings, and have not been transferred from the original placement to making them proper names indicative of the essence only, is that if a name among the names remains, the negation of the essence would not be understood, and the speaker would not become contradictory by saying 'He is existent and not knowing' as he becomes contradictory by saying 'existent and not existent.' And if the name were only a name for affirming the essence, its negation would not negate what is affirmed by its affirmation, so the essence would be negated—as if it is said 'not existent' or 'not a thing.' And since the matter is as we have established, that indicates the falsity of their statement.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalil) that the divine names are established upon reality (haqiqa) and indicate real meanings (ma'ani), not merely labels for the essence. Nasafi argues: If these names were merely proper names (a'lam) for the essence, then saying 'He is existent but not knowing' would not be contradictory, just as saying 'existent and not existent' is contradictory. But in fact, the former is considered a contradiction in the context of divine names because it implies a deficiency, whereas the latter is inherently contradictory. If a name like 'knowing' only affirmed existence ('ilm being the essence), then denying it would not remove the essence; it would only negate the label. However, when one says 'He is not knowing,' it is understood as negating a quality, not the essence, but if the name were purely for the essence, saying 'not knowing' would negate the essence itself, which is absurd. Therefore, these names must indicate additional attributes. Nasafi concludes that this demonstrates the falsehood of the opponents' claim that names do not imply attributes.

Key Terms
دليل (dalīl)
حقيقة (haqīqa)
علم ('alam)
مناقضة (munāqaḍa)
نفي (nafy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that negating a divine name like 'knowing' is logically equivalent to negating existence.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if 'knowing' were just a label for existence, then 'not knowing' would mean 'not existent', which is not what is meant. The contradiction arises only because the name indicates a real attribute beyond existence.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does saying 'He is existent but not knowing' become contradictory in the context of divine names?
A) Because 'knowing' is a necessary attribute of God.
B) Because it implies denial of the essence.
C) Because if names were mere labels, denying 'knowing' would not deny existence, but it is considered a deficiency.
D) Because the Qur'an forbids such statements.

Key Takeaway
Divine names signify real attributes because treating them as mere labels leads to logical contradictions in ordinary language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the logical impossibility of reducing Allah's names to mere labels challenge the way you actually use His names in your daily life? Do you invoke them as empty sounds or with a real awareness of the attributes they signify? What will you change today to make your recitation of the Asma' al-Husna more conscious?
?
If you truly believed that every divine name points to a real attribute of Allah, how would that change your understanding of His names during du'a and worship? Will you commit to pausing on each name you utter and reflecting on the attribute it signifies?
?
Consider the contradiction that would arise if Allah's names were just labels. Does your heart feel the weight of affirming that Allah is truly all-Knowing, all-Powerful? Or have you become comfortable with a superficial acknowledgment? What step will you take tonight to deepen that affirmation?

Al-Allaf's Claim: God's Attributes Are His Essence
Nasafi introduces al-Allaf's view identifying attributes with essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf's Mu'tazilite claim that God's attributes are identical to His essence, and distinguish it from the orthodox view of distinct attributes.

Core Idea
Al-Allaf reduces attributes to essence, denying real distinct attributes.

In this passage, al-Nasafi begins his discussion of the Mu'tazilite objections by presenting the view of their leading figure, Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf. Al-Allaf concedes that saying 'God is knowing' affirms knowledge, but he insists that this knowledge is nothing other than God's essence itself. This sets up a key theological dispute about whether attributes are distinct from the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note how al-Allaf's view is introduced.
This passage is part of a larger discussion on divine attributes.
2
Identify the attributes mentioned
The passage lists five attributes: knowledge, power, life, hearing, and sight. Al-Allaf claims each is identical to God's essence.
3
Understand the core claim
Al-Allaf agrees that calling God 'knowing' affirms knowledge, but he says that knowledge is the essence itself. This denies that attributes are distinct realities.
This view contrasts with the Sunni position that attributes are real and distinct.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why would al-Allaf make this claim? What does it imply about God's oneness? The upcoming refutation will argue against this reduction.
This connects to the lesson objective of distinguishing views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافترقت المعتزلة في الاعتراض على هذا الكلام فزعم رئيسهم الأعظم أبو الهذيل العلاف أن قولنا إن الله تعالى عالم إثبات للعلم غير أنه يزعم أن علمه ذاته وكذا قوله في قدرته وحياته وسمعه وبصره
And the Mu'tazila differed in objecting to this statement. Their great leader Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf claimed that our saying 'God is knowing' is an affirmation of knowledge, except that he claims that His knowledge is His essence. And likewise his statement regarding His power, life, hearing, and sight.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section titled 'Corruption of the Statement of al-Allaf' (Fasad Qawl al-Allaf). Nasafi states that the Mu'tazila split in their objection to the previous argument (that derived names imply attributes). Their chief, Abu Hudhayl al-Allaf, holds a specific position: He agrees that saying 'God is knowing' affirms knowledge, but he claims that this knowledge is identical to the essence (dhat) of God. Similarly, for power (qudra), life (haya), hearing (sam'), and sight (basar), he asserts they are all the essence itself. This view reduces the attributes to the essence, denying any additional eternal attributes. The passage only presents al-Allaf's claim; the refutation that follows in the text is not fully captured in this window, but the title indicates Nasafi intends to refute it.

Correction: Al-Allaf denies distinct attributes, while Ash'aris affirm they are real and distinct from the essence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Allaf's claim that God's attributes are His essence reduces them to mere identity, denying the orthodox belief in real, distinct, eternal attributes.

Proof That Divine Names Indicate Real Attributes
Nasafi argues that divine names indicate real attributes beyond the essence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi proves that divine names indicate real attributes beyond the essence, using a logical argument based on the nature of language and the principle of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Divine names are not mere labels; each name affirms a distinct attribute.

This passage contains a crucial argument from Imam Nasafi against the view that God's names are merely synonyms for His essence. Nasafi uses a linguistic principle: words are placed for specific meanings, so divine names each affirm a distinct attribute. He then provides a logical proof: if names only indicated the essence, then saying 'not knowing' would negate the essence, which it does not. Thus, names indicate real attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic principle
The passage begins with a general observation about words: some indicate absolute existence, others specific existents. Nasafi rejects the claim that a word's benefit is limited to affirming the essence; instead, each word benefits by affirming a specific meaning.
This sets up the analogy for divine names.
2
Apply the principle to divine names
Nasafi argues that each divine name adds a specific meaning beyond what another name adds. For example, 'Knowing' affirms knowledge, not just existence.
3
Follow the proof from contradiction
The proof shows that if names were only for the essence, then negating 'knowing' would negate the essence (since the name only signifies essence), but we don't see that. So the names must indicate real attributes.
This is the core argument.
4
Recognize the conclusion
Nasafi concludes that the opposing view is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعموم الموضوعة بعضها لمطلق الوجود، وبعضها لموجود مخصوص فلا تدعي أن حصول الفائدة بكل لفظ يدل على ثبوت معنى وراء الذات، بل نقول هناك زيادة الفائدة بكل لفظ لدلالته على ما وضع له كل لفظ، من إثبات زيادة خصوص في الموجود وفيما نحن فيه زيادة الفائدة أيضا لكل لفظ لدلالته على ما وضع له كل لفظ من إثبات معنى وراء ما وضع له اللفظ الآخر لدلالته عليه. وبالوقف على هذه الجملة يعرف فساد الاعتراض. والله الموفق. والدليل على أن هذه الأسامي قررت على الحقيقة وكانت دالة على المعاني، وما نقلت عن الوضع الأصلي إلى جعلها أعلاما دالة على الذات فحسب أن يبقى اسم من الأسامي لا يفهم نفي الذات ولم يصر القائل بقوله : هو موجود وليس بعالم مناقضا، كما يصير بقوله: موجود وليس بموجود، ولو كان الاسم اسما لإثبات الذات فحسب لا ينفي بنفيه ما يثبت بثبوته، فينتفي الذات – كما لو قيل ليس بموجود، أو ليس بشيء – وحيث الأمر على ما قررنا، دل ذلك على بطلان كلامهم.
And the general [nouns] placed, some for absolute existence, and some for a specific existent, so do not claim that the benefit in every word is that it indicates the affirmation of a meaning beyond the essence. Rather we say: there is additional benefit in every word due to its indication of what each word is placed for, of affirming an additional particularity in the existent. And in what we are in, there is also additional benefit for every word due to its indication of what each word is placed for, of affirming a meaning beyond what the other word is placed for, due to its indication. And by stopping at this sentence, the corruption of the objection becomes known. And God is the giver of success. And the proof that these names are established in reality and were indicative of meanings, and that they were not transferred from their original placement to being made proper names indicating only the essence, is that there remains no name among the names for which the negation of the essence is not understood, and the one who says 'He is existent and not knowing' does not become contradictory, as he becomes when he says 'existent and not existent'. And if the name were a name for affirming the essence only, its negation would not negate what it affirms by its affirmation, so the essence would be negated—as if it were said 'not existent' or 'not a thing'. And since the matter is as we have established, that indicates the falsehood of their statement.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the divine names (e.g., knowing, powerful) indicate real attributes distinct from the essence, and are not mere markers of the essence. The author begins by stating a general principle: words are placed for meanings; some are for absolute existence, some for specific existents. He then refutes a potential objection that the only benefit of a word is to affirm the essence. He argues that each word has an additional benefit: it affirms a specific meaning. In the case of divine names, each name adds a meaning beyond what another name adds. For example, 'Knowing' and 'Powerful' each affirm a specific attribute. Reflecting on this reveals the corruption of the opposing view. He then provides a proof: if names were only markers of the essence, then negating a name (e.g., saying 'He is not knowing') would not negate an attribute but only the essence, because the name only signifies the essence. But in fact, we see that saying 'He is existent and not knowing' is not a contradiction, whereas 'He is existent and not existent' is. This shows that 'knowing' is not just another word for the essence; it indicates an attribute. If it were only for the essence, then negating it would negate the essence, similar to negating 'existent'. But it does not; therefore the names indicate real attributes. This proves that the Mu'tazilite position that names are mere markers of the essence is false.

ذات
وضع
نفي
إثبات

Misconception & Correction

Correction: This passage proves they indicate distinct attributes; otherwise negating a name would negate the essence.

Checkpoint

A) Because 'knowing' is a synonym for the essence.
B) Because 'knowing' indicates a real attribute beyond the essence.
C) Because negation of names is impossible in God's case.
D) Because the essence cannot be negated.

Key Takeaway
Divine names are not mere synonyms for God's essence but indicate real, distinct attributes.

?
The Mu'tazila claimed that names are just labels for the essence. Are there any areas in your life where you reduce God's attributes to mere concepts without feeling their reality? What would it mean for your tawhid to truly believe that His names indicate real, distinct attributes?

Do you ever find yourself comfortable with a vague belief in God's existence but negligent of His specific attributes? How does this passage challenge that comfort, and what specific attribute of Allah will you meditate on today in your prayer?

Abu Hudhayl's View and the First Objection
Nasafi presents Abu Hudhayl's identity claim and the first refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hudhayl's claim that God's attributes are identical to His essence, and the first objection that this leads to the blasphemous consequence that knowledge must be worshipped.

Core Idea
If attributes are the essence, then God is knowledge, and knowledge becomes an object of worship.

This lesson examines the Mu'tazilite position of Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf, who argued that God's attributes (knowledge, power, life, etc.) are identical to His essence. Nasafi presents the first refutation: if knowledge is the essence, then the essence is knowledge, which would require worshipping knowledge—a blasphemous conclusion. We'll explore this reasoning and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Note the key claim and the objection.
The passage contains an incomplete list of names—focus on the main argument.
2
Identify Abu Hudhayl's claim
Abu Hudhayl asserts that saying 'God is knowing' affirms knowledge, but he claims that knowledge is the essence itself. The same applies to power, life, hearing, and sight.
3
Analyze the first objection
Nasafi argues: if knowledge equals the essence, then the essence is knowledge, so knowledge must be worshipped. He declares that anyone who claims God's knowledge is worshipped is a disbeliever.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: an unacceptable consequence.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Consider why identifying attributes with the essence leads to shirk (worship of something other than Allah). The objection shows that the Mu'tazilite position undermines tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافترقت المعتزلة في الاعتراض على هذا الكلام فزعم رئيسهم الأعظم أبو الهذيل العلاف أن قولنا: إن الله تعالى عالم إثبات للعلم. غير أنه يزعم أن علمه: ذاته. وكذا قوله في قدرته وحياته وسمعه وبصره. مايدل على معنيين مثل البديع بمعنى صفات ذات وبمعنى المبدع للشيء صفة فعل ومنها الجبار والجميل... إلخ. وهذا الاعتراض فاسد من وجوه أحدها: أن علمه لو كان هو ذاته لكان ذاته علما، فيجب أن يعبد علمه. أن من زعم أن علم الله تعالى يعبد فهو كافر.
And the Mu'tazila differed in objecting to this speech, and their supreme leader Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf claimed that our saying 'God is knowing' is an affirmation of knowledge. But he claims that His knowledge is His essence. And likewise his statement regarding His power, life, hearing, and sight. What indicates two meanings, like al-Badi' (the Incomparable) meaning attributes of essence and meaning the originator of things, an attribute of action, and among them al-Jabbar (the Compeller) and al-Jamil (the Beautiful) ... etc. And this objection is invalid from several aspects. One of them: that if His knowledge were His essence, then His essence would be knowledge, so His knowledge must be worshipped. And whoever claims that God's knowledge is worshipped is a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage introduces the position of Abu Hudhayl al-'Allaf, a leading Mu'tazilite, who claimed that God's attributes (knowledge, power, life, etc.) are identical to His essence. The author then presents the first objection against this view. The objection is: if knowledge is the essence, then the essence is knowledge; consequently, that knowledge would be worthy of worship. But it is blasphemous to claim that God's knowledge is worshipped. Therefore, the identity claim leads to an absurd and blasphemous consequence. The passage also includes a parenthetical remark about names like al-Badi' that can indicate two meanings (attribute of essence and attribute of action), but that remark appears incomplete in the OCR.

Correction: This objection shows that such identity leads to an absurd and blasphemous consequence: if knowledge is the essence, then the essence becomes an object of worship, which contradicts tawhid.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hudhayl's identification of attributes with the essence is refuted because it leads to the blasphemous conclusion that God's knowledge must be worshipped.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Examine your daily worship: is your love, fear, and hope directed solely to Allah, or do you find yourself worshipping a concept like 'knowledge' or 'mercy' instead of the All-Knowing, the All-Merciful?
?
Abu Hudhayl was the 'supreme leader' of his school, yet his reasoning led to a blasphemous conclusion. Whom do you trust in matters of faith? Are you willing to question a beloved teacher or scholar if their teaching contradicts the Qur'an and Sunnah? This lesson demands that you not accept any claim—even from a respected authority—without testing it against revelation and sound reasoning.
?

Do you ever casually say 'Allah's mercy' or 'Allah's knowledge' as if these are separate from Him? What steps will you take today to ensure your speech about Allah is always respectful and accurate, so that you do not fall into a similar error?
