A) He denies that God has attributes.
B) He says substance is defined by receiving attributes.
C) He says substance is defined by being a foundation for composition.
D) He says God is not capable.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argument that God would be a substance if He has attributes fails because substance is defined by being a composition substrate, not by attribute reception.

Refuting the Equating of Attributes with Accidents
Refutes the Mu'tazila equation of attribute and accident by redefining both.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between an attribute and an accident, and why God's attributes cannot be accidents.

Core Idea
Attributes distinguish essences; accidents are impermanent—God's attributes are neither.

The Mu'tazila argued that every attribute is an accident, which would make God's attributes accidents—a conclusion they themselves reject. This passage provides a clear refutation by redefining the terms: an attribute is that which provides distinction to its bearer, while an accident is a temporary occurrence in a substance. Thus, God's attributes, being eternal, are not accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the Mu'tazila claim and the author's response.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim and its dilemma
The Mu'tazila claim that every attribute is an accident. The author points out that if they hold to this, God's attributes would be accidents (which they deny), and if they deny the conclusion, they invalidate their own proof.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Understand the definition of an attribute
The author defines an attribute not by being an accident, but by providing a distinction (tamyiz) for the described essence. This is its core function.
4
Understand the definition of an accident
An accident is defined not by being an attribute, but by occurring to a substance and not enduring. Impermanence is its key feature.
The author notes that even some permanent entities are called accidents by analogy.
5
Apply the distinction to God's attributes
Since God's attributes are eternal and provide distinction, they do not fit the definition of accidents. Therefore, the Mu'tazila analogy fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قولهم: إن لا صفة في الشاهد إلا وهو عرض ولا عرض إلا وهو صفة، فإن تمسكتم بدليلكم لزمكم كون صفات الله أعراضا، وأنتم تأبون هذا، بل هو قول بعض الكرامية، وإن منعتم كونها أعراضا أبطلتم دليلكم. والجواب عنه : أن الصفة في الشاهد ما كانت صفة لأنها عرض، بل لأنها توجب تمييزا لذات الموصوف بها عن ذات لا توصف بها، والعرض ما كان عرضا لأنه صفة بل لأنه مما يعرض الجوهر ولا يدوم، ولهذا سمي ما لا دوام له من الأعيان عرضا تشبيها بالأعراض على ما مر في باب تحديد الأعراض في أول الكتاب.
And among them is their statement: There is no attribute in the witnessed world except that it is an accident, and no accident except that it is an attribute. So if you adhere to your proof, it would necessitate that God's attributes be accidents, and you reject this; indeed, it is the view of some Karramiyya. If you deny that they are accidents, you invalidate your proof. The answer to it: The attribute in the witnessed world is not an attribute because it is an accident, but because it necessitates a distinction for the essence described by it from an essence not described by it. The accident is not an accident because it is an attribute, but because it is something that occurs to the substance and does not endure. For this reason, what does not endure among entities is called an accident by analogy with accidents, as has been mentioned in the chapter on defining accidents at the beginning of the book.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazila argument that every attribute is an accident. The answer distinguishes between attribute and accident: attribute is what provides distinction to the described essence, while accident is what occurs to a substance and is impermanent. Thus, God's attributes are not accidents because they are not temporary.

Key Terms
صفة
عرض
تمييز
جوهر
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attribute and accident are synonymous; every attribute is an accident.
Correction: Attribute and accident have different defining features: attribute provides distinction, accident is impermanent. Thus, not every attribute is an accident, especially God's eternal attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what makes something an attribute in the witnessed world?
A) Because it is an accident.
B) Because it is impermanent.
C) Because it provides distinction to the described essence.
D) Because it occurs to a substance.

Key Takeaway
Attributes distinguish essences; accidents are impermanent—God's eternal attributes are not accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever looked at a flaw in a human attribute—like a friend's failing memory—and then, in your heart, doubted that God's knowledge could be perfect and permanent? How does this lesson's distinction between attribute and accident challenge that doubt, and what will you do the next time that doubt surfaces?

The Mu'tazila made an honest intellectual error by equating attribute with accident. In what area of your life are you tempted to measure God's nature by the created things you see? Is there a specific situation where you have expected God to act like a human, or to have the same limitations? Commit today to ask God to purify your understanding of His attributes.
?
If attributes are defined by distinction (tamyīz), then God's attributes make Him utterly unique. Are there any ways you have inadvertently blurred the line between God's attributes and something in creation—for example, by thinking of His mercy as exactly like a parent's mercy? What practical step can you take this week to study one of God's names (like Al-ʿAlīm or Ar-Raḥmān) and let that distinction transform your worship?

The General Principle for Refuting Mu'tazila Arguments on Divine Attributes
States the general principle for refuting Mu'tazila arguments about divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi school refutes Mu'tazila arguments by distinguishing essential attributes from accidental properties.

Core Idea
Knowledge is defined by its essence, not by contingent features.

The Mu'tazila argue that if God had knowledge, it would resemble human knowledge—impermanent, accidental, and acquired. This lesson presents the Maturidi response: those properties are not what make knowledge knowledge. The refutation demonstrates that the nature of a thing is determined by its essence, not by accidental attributes shared with other things.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazila list properties they claim would apply to God's knowledge: impermanence, being an accident, being of the genus of inner thoughts, and being necessary or acquired. This sets up the challenge.
Notice how they assume that if God knows, His knowledge must be like human knowledge.
2
Learn the refutation point by point
Follow the response: each property is examined and shown not to define knowledge. For example, impermanence is shared with ignorance, which is not knowledge. The commentator shows that sharing an accidental property does not make something knowledge.
The key insight: knowledge is defined by its essence, not by these shared properties.
3
Grasp the underlying principle
Understand the general principle: the nature of a thing is not defined by contingent attributes that it may share with other things. This allows God's knowledge to be eternal and necessary without contradiction.
4
Apply the principle to other arguments
The passage concludes that any similar argument from the Mu'tazila can be repelled using this same method. Reflect on how this principle can be extended to other divine attributes.
The principle is general: do not confuse accidental properties with essential definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجميع ما يموه المعتزلة على الضعفة يخرج على هذا الأصل، من نحو قولهم: لو كان الله تعالى علم لكان مستحيل البقاء، ولكان عرضا ولكان من جنس الضمائر والاعتقادات، ولكان ضروريا أو مكتسبا، ولا يعقل علم يخرج عن هذه المعاني. فإنا نقول: العلم ما كان علما لأنه مستحيل البقاء لمشاركة الجهل وجميع الأعراض إياه فيه، وليست بعلم، ولا لأنه عرض لهذا أيضا، ولا لكونه من جنس الضمائر والعقائد، فإن الجهل والظن والشك يشاركه المرتعش ضرورة وليست بعلم، وكذا حركات العروق النابضة ولا لكونه مكتسبا، لأن الحركات الاختيارية، والسكون الإرادي كلها مكتسبة وليست بعلم، والعلم الحاصل بالحواس الخمس والبداءة ليست بمكتسبة وهي ما علوم. وجميع يورد من هذا القبيل يدفع على هذا السبيل، والله ولي التوفيق.
All that the Mu'tazila obscure to the weak falls under this principle, such as their saying: 'If God were knowing, He would be impossible of endurance, and would be an accident, and would be of the genus of inner thoughts and beliefs, and would be necessary or acquired, and no knowledge is conceivable that escapes these meanings.' For we say: Knowledge is not knowledge because it is impossible of endurance, sharing with ignorance and all accidents in that while they are not knowledge; nor because it is an accident as well; nor because it is of the genus of inner thoughts and beliefs, for ignorance, conjecture, and doubt share with it in trembling necessity [؟] and are not knowledge; likewise the movements of pulsating veins; nor because it is acquired, for voluntary movements and intentional stillness are all acquired and are not knowledge; and knowledge obtained through the five senses and intuition is not acquired and not [؟] sciences. Everything that is brought up of this category is repelled in this way. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage applies a general principle: the nature of a thing is not defined by contingent properties. The Mu'tazila argue that if God had knowledge, it would be like human knowledge, which is impermanent, an accident, etc. The refutation shows that those properties are not what make knowledge knowledge; rather, knowledge is that which is known, and it can be permanent in God. Examples are given to illustrate that sharing certain properties does not make something knowledge.

Key Terms
علم
عرض
مستحيل البقاء
مكتسب
ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that attributes of creatures must be identical in nature to attributes of the Creator.
Correction: Attributes are defined by their essence, not by accidental properties. Therefore, God's knowledge can be eternal and necessary without sharing the limitations of human knowledge.

Checkpoint

B) Because it is an accident.
C) Because it is acquired.
D) Because it is known.

Key Takeaway
The nature of a thing is defined by its essence, not by accidental properties; thus God's knowledge is eternal and necessary, unlike human knowledge.

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Inseparability of Attributes
Refutes the Mu'tazila claim of non-observation in the created world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila objection that the inseparability of attributes from essence is not observed in the created world, and the beginning of the Maturidi response based on the meaning of 'knowing'.

Core Idea
Knowledge inheres in the heart, yet the whole body is called 'knowing' — showing that inherence is not required for description.

The Mu'tazila raise a clever objection: if attributes are inseparable from God's essence, why do we see in creation that a person is called 'knowing' even though knowledge is only in the heart, not the whole body? This passage presents the objection and begins the response by clarifying how the name 'knowing' is derived from the meaning, not from the attribute's inherence in every part. The discussion highlights key principles of how we speak about attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to understand the Mu'tazila claim: that the inseparability of attribute and essence is not witnessed in creation. Notice the example of knowledge and the heart.
The objection seems plausible at first glance.
2
Identify the two sources of error
The author then lists two common sources of error in reasoning. Find these two and understand why they lead to mistakes.
These are general logical fallacies applied here.
3
Follow the beginning of the reply
The reply starts by showing that the name 'knowing' is derived from the meaning (knowledge), not from the attribute's inherence in every part. Consider how this undermines the objection.
The response is cut off, but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا ما قدمتم من المقدمة مقدمة صادقة وما مهدتموه من القاعدة قاعدة مسلمة. غير أن دعوى عدم الانفكاك في الشاهد دعوى ممنوعة فإنا لا نسلم أن الاتصاف بكونه عالما، لا ينفك عن قيام الصفة بالذات بل لا يجتمعان في الشاهد أبدا، فإن العلم قيامه بالقلب ولا يوصف هو بكونه عالما والعالم جميع البدن ولا علم في أكثر البدن بل العلم في جزء منه دون سائره، فما ذكرتم من الدليل صحيح، غير أنه لا وجود له في المتنازع فيه، وأكثر الغلط يثبت من أمرين أحدهما جعل ما ليس بدليل دليلا، وإن وجد في المتنازع فيه. والآخر: ادعاء وجود ما هو الدليل حقيقة في محل لا وجود له فيه، فكان من حق المجادل أن يعرف العناية إلى التمييز بين ما هو دليل وبين ما ليس بدليل، ثم إلى وجوده في المتنازع فيه لو كان دليلا، ولا وجود ها هنا لهذا الدليل. قيل لهم : لا شك أن العالم اسم مأخوذ من المعنى على ما تقدم يدل عليه أن العلم لو انعدم عن القلب لا يوصف الذات بكونه عالما، وإذا حصل في القلب يوصف به. ونحن لم نشترط للاتصاف قيام العلم به بل شرطنا أن يكون للعلم
For if they say: The premise you presented is a true premise and the foundation you laid is an accepted foundation. However, the claim of non-separation in the witnessed world is a forbidden claim, for we do not accept that the description of being knowing is inseparable from the inherence of the attribute in the essence; rather, they never occur together in the witnessed world. For knowledge inheres in the heart, and the heart is not described as knowing, while the 'knower' is the whole body, and there is no knowledge in most of the body; rather, knowledge is in a part of it without the rest. So the proof you mentioned is correct, but it has no existence in the matter under dispute. Most error arises from two things: one is making what is not a proof into a proof, and even if it exists in the matter under dispute. The other is claiming the existence of what is actually the proof in a place where it does not exist. So it is the duty of the disputant to know regarding distinguishing between what is a proof and what is not a proof, then regarding its existence in the matter under dispute if it were a proof, and there is no existence here for this proof. It was said to them: There is no doubt that the name 'knowing' is taken from the meaning, as previously shown, because if knowledge were removed from the heart, the essence would not be described as knowing, and when it occurs in the heart, it is described by it. And we did not condition for the description the inherence of knowledge in it; rather, we conditioned that knowledge [be for the...]
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage addresses another Mu'tazila objection: the doctrine that God's attributes are inseparable from His essence is not observed in the created world, where a person can be described as knowing even though knowledge does not inhere in the whole body but only in the heart. The answer clarifies that the name 'knowing' is derived from the meaning (knowledge), and the description does not require that the knowledge inhere in the entire entity. The author then begins to state that they did not condition description upon direct inherence, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
عدم الانفكاك
اتصاف
قيام الصفة
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that description of a thing requires the attribute to inhere in every part of it.
Correction: In the created world, a person is called 'knowing' even though knowledge only inheres in the heart, not the whole body. Therefore, description can be based on the meaning, not on universal inherence.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the Mu'tazila objection regarding 'عدم الانفكاك'?
A) They argue that knowledge is not an attribute.
B) They argue that the claim of inseparability is not observed in the created world.
C) They argue that God cannot be described as knowing.
D) They argue that knowledge is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The inseparability of attributes from essence is not contradicted by the created world, because attribution follows the meaning, not the attribute's inherence in every part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on Allah's attributes, do you ever catch yourself thinking of them as similar to human qualities, just 'bigger'? How does the lesson's distinction between attribution and inherence challenge that tendency in your own heart?
?
The Mu'tazila made an error by assuming what is true in creation must apply to the Creator. In your own life, where are you tempted to judge Allah's actions or attributes by your limited human experience? Will you commit today to submitting to what He has revealed rather than your own reasoning?
?
The passage warns that most errors come from either using a false proof or using a true proof in the wrong place. Think of a belief you hold about Allah — is it based on solid evidence from revelation? Or have you unconsciously adopted it from culture or personal preference? What step can you take this week to ground your beliefs more firmly in the Qur'an and Sunnah?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
The Knower Possesses Knowledge
Author defines knower and argues knowledge belongs to the whole essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the knower (al-'alim) is defined as one who possesses knowledge rather than one in whom knowledge subsists, and that knowledge cannot be separated from the knower.

Core Idea
The knower is the possessor of knowledge, not its locus.

This lesson establishes the foundational definition of the knower in relation to knowledge. The author argues that when knowledge subsists in a part of an essence, it belongs to the whole essence. This leads to the conclusion that there is no knowledge without a knower and no knower without knowledge, contrary to the Mu'tazila who affirm a knower without knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the definition of the knower
Carefully read the Arabic and translation to understand the author's initial thesis: the knower is the one who has knowledge, not the one in whom knowledge subsists.
Notice the logical structure using 'fā' (so) to connect statements.
2
Understand the condition for being qualified
The author explains that subsistence in the essence is not a condition for being described by the attribute; rather, it is necessary for the description to belong to the thing. If knowledge subsisted in another locus or no locus, it would not belong to the thing.
This refutes a potential misunderstanding about attribute attribution.
3
Consider the relational names analogy
The author uses the example of father and son to show that attributes can belong without subsistence in the locus. The father is called father even though the son does not subsist in him, because of evidence of specificity.
This strengthens the argument that knowledge can belong to the whole even if subsisting in a part.
4
Identify the Mu'tazila error
The passage ends by stating that the Mu'tazila affirm a knower without knowledge at all, which is the error being addressed.
This sets up the need for further clarification in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللعالم علم، فإذا العالم من له العلم لا من قام به العلم، فإذا قام العلم ببعضه كان العلم لكل الذات، فإذا كل الذات له العلم فكان عالما، وقيام الصفة بالذات لا يشترط للاتصاف لأن الشرط هو القيام بل ليكون الوصف له إذ لو قام في محل آخر أو لا في محل لم يكن الوصف له، ولهذا أبينا أن يكون الباري جل وعلا مريدا بإرادة حادثة لا في محل كما ذهب إليه الجبائي وابنه، لأن الإرادة لا تكون له لانعدام دليل الاختصاص، فأما إذا قام العلم بالبعض كان علما لهذا الذات، لأن جميع الذات كان إنسانا واحدا، وعده شيئا واحدا بجعل الله تعالى كذلك، فوصف كل الذات بكونه عالما لأن له العلم، ولهذا تسمى الذات في الأسماء الإضافية كالأب والابن والزوج والزوجة باعتبار ما ليس بقائم بذاته، فإن الابن ليس بقائم بذات الأب الذي يسمى لأجله أبا ، ولكن لما ثبت له دليل الاختصاص سمي به، فكذا في هذه الأسماء، فإذا لا انفكاك للعلم عن العالم ولا للعالم عن العلم، ولا علم إلا لعالم ولا عالم بدون العلم. والمعتزلة يثبتون العالم بدون العلم أصلا، وإلى هذا الجواب ذهب كثير من متكلمي أصحابنا، ويقولون : إن نفع العلم ونقيضه الجهل راجع إلى كل الذات، وكذا القدرة والعجز والسمع والصمم، والبصر والعمى، وكذا البطش والمشي، والدليل عليه أن محال هذه المعاني جعلت ملحقة بالآلات لكل البدن، وجعل الموصوف بها كل البدن، فيقال: نظرت ببصري وسمعت بأذني، ومشيت إلي فلان بقدمي، وكتبت الخط بيدي وهذا جواب واضح. وكثير من متأخري أصحاب الصفات يقولون: إن العلم في الحقيقة صفة الجزء الذي قام به، وكذا الكلام في القدرة والسمع والبصر فذلك الجزء هو العالم لا ما سواه من البدن، وكذا في جميع الصفات وما يقال: فلان عالم معناه عالم القلب إلا أن ذكر القلب أسقط لوضوح المعنى طلبا للتخفيف كما هو دأب أهل اللسان حتى إن الصفة لو كانت تقوم ببعض الأعضاء ولسائر الأعضاء في كونها صالحة لقبول تلك الصفة مشاركة، وإمكان القبول لكل متحقق لابد من ذكر المحل لانعدام دلالة التعين لدى الازدحام، فيقال: فلان عظيم الرأس أو عظيم البطن أو واسع الفم وعريض الجبهة، وكذا يقال: فلان واسع الصدر نافذ البصيرة، صائب الرأي ماض العزيمة، قوي الصريمة، شديد الشكيمة، فكان الاعتراض على رأي من يجيب بهذا الجواب في غاية الوهاء. والله الموفق والمعين.
And for the world there is knowledge. So the knower is the one who has knowledge, not the one in whom knowledge subsists. For if knowledge subsists in some part of him, then knowledge belongs to the entire essence. So the entire essence has knowledge, so it is knowing. The subsistence of the attribute in the essence is not a condition for being qualified, because the condition is the subsistence itself, but rather for the description to belong to it. For if it subsisted in another locus or in no locus, the description would not belong to it. For this reason we have refused that the Exalted Creator be willing by a will that is originated not in a locus, as al-Jubba'i and his son held, because the will would not belong to Him due to the absence of evidence of specificity. But if knowledge subsists in some part, it is knowledge for this essence, because the whole essence was one human being, and it is considered one thing by the making of God the Exalted. So the entire essence is described as knowing because it has knowledge. For this reason the essence is named in relational names like father, son, husband, wife, in consideration of what does not subsist in its essence. For the son does not subsist in the essence of the father, who is called father because of him, but when evidence of specificity is established, he is named by it. So likewise in these names. Thus there is no separation of knowledge from the knower nor of the knower from knowledge. There is no knowledge except for a knower, and no knower without knowledge. The Mu'tazila affirm a knower without knowledge at all. Many of the theologians of our companions have adopted this answer and say: The benefit of knowledge and its opposite, ignorance, pertains to the entire essence; similarly power and incapacity, hearing and deafness, sight and blindness, similarly strength and walking. The evidence for that is that the loci of these meanings are connected to the instruments of the whole body, and the one described by them is the whole body. So it is said: I looked with my eye, I heard with my ear, I walked to so-and-so with my foot, I wrote the letter with my hand. This is a clear answer. Many of the later scholars of the attributes say: Knowledge in reality is an attribute of the part in which it subsists, and similarly the discussion regarding power, hearing, and sight. That part is the knower, not the rest of the body. Likewise in all attributes. And what is said: So-and-so is knowing means 'the heart is knowing', except that mentioning the heart is omitted due to the clarity of the meaning, seeking brevity, as is the practice of the people of language. So much so that if the attribute were to subsist in some organs while the other organs share in being fit for accepting that attribute, and the possibility of acceptance is realized for each, it is necessary to mention the locus due to the absence of indication of specificity when there is crowding. So it is said: So-and-so has a large head or a large belly or a wide mouth and broad forehead, and likewise it is said: So-and-so is broad-chested, penetrating insight, correct opinion, firm resolve, strong determination, severe sternness. So the objection against the opinion of those who answer with this answer is extremely weak. And Allah is the Grantor of success and the Helper.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of the attribute of knowledge (al-'ilm) and its relationship to the essence (al-dhat). The author begins by defining the knower (al-'alim) as the one who possesses knowledge, not the one in whom knowledge subsists as a separate entity. He explains that when knowledge subsists in a part of an essence, it accrues to the whole essence because the essence is considered one unified thing by God's making. Therefore, the entire essence is described as knowing. The author then addresses a potential objection regarding relational names like father and son, showing that even though the son does not subsist in the father's essence, the father is still called father due to a special relation. Similarly, the attribute of knowledge is truly attributed to the whole essence even if it subsists only in a part. The Mu'tazila are criticized for affirming a knower without knowledge at all. The author presents two views among his own school: first, that the benefit of knowledge pertains to the whole body, as evidenced by common speech (e.g., 'I looked with my eye'), and second, that knowledge is actually an attribute of a specific part (the heart), but the mention of that part is omitted for brevity in ordinary language. The author then uses analogies from physical attributes (large head, broad chest) to show that when a quality is shared among multiple organs, the specific locus is mentioned to avoid ambiguity. He concludes that the objection to the second view is extremely weak.

Key Terms
العلم
العالم
الذات
الصفة
دليل الاختصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowledge can be an accidental attribute separate from the knower, or that a knower can exist without knowledge.
Correction: The author argues that there is no separation; the knower is defined by having knowledge, and knowledge cannot exist without a knower.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what defines the knower (al-'alim)?
A) The one in whom knowledge subsists as a separate entity.
B) The one who has knowledge.
C) The one who is a locus for knowledge.
D) The one who is independent of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The knower is defined by having knowledge, and knowledge cannot be separated from the knower.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is the All-Knowing (العليم) and that His knowledge is inseparable from His essence, how does that change the way you approach your own pursuit of knowledge? Are you seeking knowledge as an attribute of yourself, or are you content with superficial learning that does not penetrate your heart? What will you do this week to ensure your knowledge becomes real?
?
The passage argues that a knower cannot exist without knowledge. In your own life, is there an area where you claim to 'know' something (e.g., that Allah is watching, that the Quran is true) but your actions contradict that claimed knowledge? Confront that contradiction and decide on one specific action to align your behavior with your professed knowledge.
?
The Mu'tazila were criticized for affirming a knower without knowledge. Are there areas of faith where you have a 'knower without knowledge'—i.e., you claim to believe something but lack the profound, transformative knowledge that should accompany it? What step will you take this week to turn that claimed knowledge into real, lived knowledge?

First Response: Knowledge Benefits the Whole Body
Author presents first response: knowledge benefits the whole essence as shown by common speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first response to the Mu'tazila: that the benefit of knowledge and other attributes pertains to the entire essence, as evidenced by common speech about bodily actions.

Core Idea
The benefit of knowledge extends to the whole essence, as shown by common speech.

After stating the Mu'tazila error, the author presents the first response from many of his school's theologians. They argue that the benefit of knowledge and its opposite, ignorance, pertain to the entire essence, just as actions like seeing and hearing are attributed to the whole body even though they involve specific organs. This is supported by linguistic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the first response passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the core claim: the benefit of knowledge pertains to the whole essence.
Notice the list of attributes: knowledge, power, hearing, sight, etc.
2
Understand the linguistic evidence
The author provides examples from common speech: 'I looked with my eye,' 'I heard with my ear.' These show that actions are attributed to the whole person, not just the organ.
This reinforces the idea that attributes benefit the entire essence.
3
Connect to the Mu'tazila objection
This response directly counters the Mu'tazila claim that a knower can exist without knowledge, by showing that knowledge's effects are inseparable from the whole being.
The response is considered a clear answer by the author.

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of the attribute of knowledge (al-'ilm) and its relationship to the essence (al-dhat). The author begins by defining the knower (al-'alim) as the one who possesses knowledge, not the one in whom knowledge subsists as a separate entity. ?

?

Second Response: Knowledge Resides in a Specific Locus
Author presents second response: knowledge actually in a part, omission for brevity, supported by analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second response: that knowledge actually resides in a specific part (the heart) but this is omitted for brevity in speech, and that mentioning the locus is necessary when there is ambiguity.

Core Idea
Knowledge resides in the heart, but the locus is omitted for brevity in common speech.

The author presents an alternative response from later scholars of his school: knowledge is really an attribute of a specific part (the heart), but in ordinary language, the mention of that part is omitted for brevity. This is supported by analogies from physical attributes where the locus is mentioned when needed to avoid confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the second response passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the claim that knowledge is in a specific part.
The author mentions the heart as the locus.
2
Understand the omission for brevity
When we say 'so-and-so is knowing,' it means 'his heart is knowing.' The heart is omitted because the meaning is clear without it.
This is a linguistic principle: speakers omit what is obvious.
3
Examine the physical attribute analogies
The author gives examples: when an attribute could be shared by multiple organs, the specific locus is mentioned (e.g., 'wide mouth,' 'broad forehead'). This shows that when there is no ambiguity, omission is allowed.
This supports the idea that knowledge's locus can be omitted because the heart is uniquely suited for it.
4
Assess the objection
The author concludes that the objection to this view is extremely weak.
The passage ends with a assertion of its strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللعالم علم، فإذا العالم من له العلم لا من قام به العلم، فإذا قام العلم ببعضه كان العلم لكل الذات، فإذا كل الذات له العلم فكان عالما، وقيام الصفة بالذات لا يشترط للاتصاف لأن الشرط هو القيام بل ليكون الوصف له إذ لو قام في محل آخر أو لا في محل لم يكن الوصف له، ولهذا أبينا أن يكون الباري جل وعلا مريدا بإرادة حادثة لا في محل كما ذهب إليه الجبائي وابنه، لأن الإرادة لا تكون له لانعدام دليل الاختصاص، فأما إذا قام العلم بالبعض كان علما لهذا الذات، لأن جميع الذات كان إنسانا واحدا، وعده شيئا واحدا بجعل الله تعالى كذلك، فوصف كل الذات بكونه عالما لأن له العلم، ولهذا تسمى الذات في الأسماء الإضافية كالأب والابن والزوج والزوجة باعتبار ما ليس بقائم بذاته، فإن الابن ليس بقائم بذات الأب الذي يسمى لأجله أبا ، ولكن لما ثبت له دليل الاختصاص سمي به، فكذا في هذه الأسماء، فإذا لا انفكاك للعلم عن العالم ولا للعالم عن العلم، ولا علم إلا لعالم ولا عالم بدون العلم. والمعتزلة يثبتون العالم بدون العلم أصلا، وإلى هذا الجواب ذهب كثير من متكلمي أصحابنا، ويقولون : إن نفع العلم ونقيضه الجهل راجع إلى كل الذات، وكذا القدرة والعجز والسمع والصمم، والبصر والعمى، وكذا البطش والمشي، والدليل عليه أن محال هذه المعاني جعلت ملحقة بالآلات لكل البدن، وجعل الموصوف بها كل البدن، فيقال: نظرت ببصري وسمعت بأذني، ومشيت إلي فلان بقدمي، وكتبت الخط بيدي وهذا جواب واضح. وكثير من متأخري أصحاب الصفات يقولون: إن العلم في الحقيقة صفة الجزء الذي قام به، وكذا الكلام في القدرة والسمع والبصر فذلك الجزء هو العالم لا ما سواه من البدن، وكذا في جميع الصفات وما يقال: فلان عالم معناه عالم القلب إلا أن ذكر القلب أسقط لوضوح المعنى طلبا للتخفيف كما هو دأب أهل اللسان حتى إن الصفة لو كانت تقوم ببعض الأعضاء ولسائر الأعضاء في كونها صالحة لقبول تلك الصفة مشاركة، وإمكان القبول لكل متحقق لابد من ذكر المحل لانعدام دلالة التعين لدى الازدحام، فيقال: فلان عظيم الرأس أو عظيم البطن أو واسع الفم وعريض الجبهة، وكذا يقال: فلان واسع الصدر نافذ البصيرة، صائب الرأي ماض العزيمة، قوي الصريمة، شديد الشكيمة، فكان الاعتراض على رأي من يجيب بهذا الجواب في غاية الوهاء. والله الموفق والمعين.
And for the world there is knowledge. So the knower is the one who has knowledge, not the one in whom knowledge subsists. For if knowledge subsists in some part of him, then knowledge belongs to the entire essence. So the entire essence has knowledge, so it is knowing. The subsistence of the attribute in the essence is not a condition for being qualified, because the condition is the subsistence itself, but rather for the description to belong to it. For if it subsisted in another locus or in no locus, the description would not belong to it. For this reason we have refused that the Exalted Creator be willing by a will that is originated not in a locus, as al-Jubba'i and his son held, because the will would not belong to Him due to the absence of evidence of specificity. But if knowledge subsists in some part, it is knowledge for this essence, because the whole essence was one human being, and it is considered one thing by the making of God the Exalted. So the entire essence is described as knowing because it has knowledge. For this reason the essence is named in relational names like father, son, husband, wife, in consideration of what does not subsist in its essence. For the son does not subsist in the essence of the father, who is called father because of him, but when evidence of specificity is established, he is named by it. So likewise in these names. Thus there is no separation of knowledge from the knower nor of the knower from knowledge. There is no knowledge except for a knower, and no knower without knowledge. The Mu'tazila affirm a knower without knowledge at all. Many of the theologians of our companions have adopted this answer and say: The benefit of knowledge and its opposite, ignorance, pertains to the entire essence; similarly power and incapacity, hearing and deafness, sight and blindness, similarly strength and walking. The evidence for that is that the loci of these meanings are connected to the instruments of the whole body, and the one described by them is the whole body. So it is said: I looked with my eye, I heard with my ear, I walked to so-and-so with my foot, I wrote the letter with my hand. This is a clear answer. Many of the later scholars of the attributes say: Knowledge in reality is an attribute of the part in which it subsists, and similarly the discussion regarding power, hearing, and sight. That part is the knower, not the rest of the body. Likewise in all attributes. And what is said: So-and-so is knowing means 'the heart is knowing', except that mentioning the heart is omitted due to the clarity of the meaning, seeking brevity, as is the practice of the people of language. So much so that if the attribute were to subsist in some organs while the other organs share in being fit for accepting that attribute, and the possibility of acceptance is realized for each, it is necessary to mention the locus due to the absence of indication of specificity when there is crowding. So it is said: So-and-so has a large head or a large belly or a wide mouth and broad forehead, and likewise it is said: So-and-so is broad-chested, penetrating insight, correct opinion, firm resolve, strong determination, severe sternness. So the objection against the opinion of those who answer with this answer is extremely weak. And Allah is the Grantor of success and the Helper.
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the nature of the attribute of knowledge (al-'ilm) and its relationship to the essence (al-dhat). The author begins by defining the knower (al-'alim) as the one who possesses knowledge, not the one in whom knowledge subsists as a separate entity. He explains that when knowledge subsists in a part of an essence, it accrues to the whole essence because the essence is considered one unified thing by God's making. Therefore, the entire essence is described as knowing. The author then addresses a potential objection regarding relational names like father and son, showing that even though the son does not subsist in the father's essence, the father is still called father due to a special relation. Similarly, the attribute of knowledge is truly attributed to the whole essence even if it subsists only in a part. The Mu'tazila are criticized for affirming a knower without knowledge at all. The author presents two views among his own school: first, that the benefit of knowledge pertains to the whole body, as evidenced by common speech (e.g., 'I looked with my eye'), and second, that knowledge is actually an attribute of a specific part (the heart), but the mention of that part is omitted for brevity in ordinary language. The author then uses analogies from physical attributes (large head, broad chest) to show that when a quality is shared among multiple organs, the specific locus is mentioned to avoid ambiguity. He concludes that the objection to the second view is extremely weak.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowledge does not reside in any specific part of the body, or that the whole body is equally the locus of knowledge.
Correction: Knowledge actually resides in the heart, but the mention is omitted for brevity in common speech.

Checkpoint
According to the later scholars of attributes, why is the mention of the heart omitted in the phrase 'so-and-so is knowing'?
A) Because the heart is not the locus of knowledge.
B) Because the meaning is clear without it, and speakers seek brevity.
C) Because the heart is not a physical organ.
D) Because the heart is mentioned only in poetic language.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge resides in the heart, but the locus is omitted for brevity; mentioning it is necessary only when ambiguity arises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I know Islam,' do you perceive that knowledge as residing in your head or in your heart? What would change if you truly lived from the conviction that your heart is the real knower?
?
If you were to silently ask your heart right now about your most deeply held belief, what would it answer? Are you prepared to listen and obey that answer today?
?
The author argues that mentioning the heart is omitted for brevity, but do you ever overlook this truth in your daily life? Name one specific way you will actively remind yourself that your knowledge—and therefore your faith—must emanate from your heart, not just your mind.

Proof of God's Knowledge from Precision of Creation
The author argues that precision indicates knowledge, with an analogy and a note on the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the precision and perfection of created things indicate both the power and knowledge of the Creator.

Core Idea
The precision of creation proves the Creator's knowledge, not just His power.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a foundational argument of Islamic theology: the observable perfection of the universe points not only to a powerful Maker but to a knowing One. He uses a vivid everyday analogy to show that denying this would be irrational. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the concise style of the author.
2
Identify the main claim and analogy
The author states that the effect indicates the agent, and precision indicates knowledge. He uses the example of a decorated brocade or a luxurious house built with perfection.
This analogy is key to understanding the argument.
3
Understand how precision indicates knowledge
The author argues that anyone who sees a perfectly crafted object immediately infers that its maker has knowledge. Denying this would be foolish or obstinate.
Consider why precision without knowledge is impossible.
4
Consider the Mu'tazila's dilemma
The author notes that the Mu'tazila have three options, one of which is to fully equate the witnessed and unseen, which would force them to accept the proof of knowledge.
This shows how the argument challenges their doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفعول يدل على الفاعل والإحكام يدل على العلم. ولنا أيضا طريقة دلالة المحدثات على الصفات، وهي أن المفعول كما دل على الفاعل، فمطلقه يدل على القدرة وكونه محكما متقنا يدل على العلم فإن كل من رأى المفعول محكما متقنا استدل بكونه مفعولا على قدرة فاعله عليه، وبكونه محكما على علم فاعله به حتى إن من زعم أن ديباجا منقشا أو دارا فاخرة فيها نقوش وتصاوير، وهي مبنية على غاية الإحكام والإتقان عاجز عنها، استجهل ونسب إما إلى الحماقة وإما إلى العناد والمكابرة. كما أن من زعم أن ذلك كله حصل بنفسه من غير ناسج ولا بأن نسب إلى ذلك. ثم بعد هذه المقدمة كانت المعتزلة بين أمور ثلاثة: فإما أن يسووا بين الشاهد والغائب في كل ذلك، ويقولون بأن قدرة الصانع ثابتة بدلالة المفعول، وعلمه ثابت بدلالة إحكام المفعول وإتقانه، كما أن ذاته ثابت بدلالة المفعولات عليه كما في الشاهد، وفي هذا ترك مذهبهم، لكنه انقياد للحق وإذعان للدليل، واعتراف بثبوت المدلول عند قيام دليل ثبوته
The effect indicates the agent, and precision indicates knowledge. We also have the method of the indication of originated things for the attributes, which is that the effect, just as it indicates the agent, so its absolute indicates power, and its being precise and perfected indicates knowledge. For everyone who sees an effect precise and perfected infers, by its being an effect, the power of its agent upon it, and by its being precise, the knowledge of its agent of it. So much so that whoever claims that a decorated brocade or a luxurious house containing engravings and pictures, built to the utmost precision and perfection, is beyond the capability of its agent, is deemed foolish and attributed either to stupidity or to obstinacy and defiance. Just as whoever claims that all that came about by itself without a weaver or builder is attributed to that. Then after this introduction, the Mu'tazila are among three matters: Either they equalize between the witnessed and the absent in all that, and say that the power of the Maker is established by the indication of the effect, and His knowledge is established by the indication of the effect's precision and perfection, just as His essence is established by the indication of effects upon Him as in the witnessed. In this there is abandonment of their doctrine, but it is submission to truth and yielding to evidence, and acknowledgment of the establishment of the indicated when the evidence for its establishment is present.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage is a proof of God's attributes of power (al-qudra) and knowledge (al-'ilm) from the created world. The author presents a general principle: an effect (al-maf'ul) points to its agent (al-fa'il), and the precision (al-ihkam) and perfection (al-itqan) of the effect point to the agent's knowledge. He illustrates this with the example of a decorated brocade or a luxurious house: anyone who sees such a well-made object immediately recognizes that it requires a powerful and knowledgeable maker. To deny that would be foolish or obstinate, just as it would be absurd to claim that such things came about by themselves. The author then notes that the Mu'tazila face three possible positions (though only the first is elaborated due to the text's cutoff). The first is to completely equate the witnessed (al-shahid) with the unseen (al-gha'ib) and accept that both power and knowledge are proven by the effect and its precision, just as the essence is proven. The author comments that this would mean abandoning the Mu'tazilite position (which typically denies eternal attributes) but is actually submission to truth.

الفاعل
الإحكام
الإتقان
القدرة
العلم
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the precision of creation only proves a powerful creator, but not necessarily a knowledgeable one.
Correction: The author explicitly argues that the precision and perfection of the effect indicate the agent's knowledge, not just his power, using clear reasoning and analogy.

Checkpoint
What do the precision (إحكام) and perfection (إتقان) of an effect primarily indicate about its agent?
A) The agent's power only.
B) The agent's knowledge.
C) The agent's existence.
D) The agent's eternity.

Key Takeaway
The precision and perfection of the created world are clear evidence that its Creator possesses knowledge, just as the existence of the world proves His power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at the precision in your own body and the world around you. Do you truly believe that every detail is known by God? If so, why do you still fall into carelessness in your actions, as if they are not seen by an All-Knowing Creator? What change must you make today to live with that awareness?
?
The author says denying precision is stupidity or obstinacy. Where in your life do you find yourself making excuses instead of acknowledging God's perfect knowledge? Be honest—what specific area are you avoiding?
?
If you truly internalized that God's knowledge is evident in every precise thing, how would that change your fear of Him and your hope in His mercy? What is one practical step you will take today to remind yourself of that?

Proof from Perfection of Creation for Creator's Knowledge
A proof from creation's perfection for the Creator's knowledge and analysis of Mu'tazilite responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perfection and soundness of created things indicate the Creator's knowledge, and why the Mu'tazilites' responses lead to contradiction.

Core Idea
Perfection in creation points to knowledge in the Creator.

This passage presents a compelling argument from the observed perfection of the universe. Just as a finely crafted object unmistakably indicates a skilled maker, the flawless order and precision in creation point to the Creator's perfect knowledge. The author then exposes the inevitable contradictions faced by the Mu'tazilites who deny eternal attributes, showing that any attempt to limit the analogy between this world and the unseen leads to inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how the author builds the argument from a simple analogy to a trilemma.
The passage is dense but structured. Pay attention to the three options presented.
2
Understand the core analogy
The author argues that an effect indicates its agent's power and knowledge. Just as a sound and perfect effect implies a knowledgeable agent in daily experience, the perfection of creation implies the Creator's knowledge.
The examples of an embroidered brocade and a fine house illustrate the point.
3
Analyze the three Mu'tazilite positions
The Mu'tazilites are presented with three options: full analogy (affirming attributes), full differentiation (denying everything), or partial analogy (affirming only essence). Each has consequences.
The third option is highlighted as contradictory on two counts.
4
Reflect on the contradictions
The partial analogy creates inconsistency: why accept the indication for essence but not for attributes? Why differentiate between witnessed and unseen for attributes but not for essence?
5
Conclude with the author's method
The author affirms reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen when there is equivalence, and submission to evidence.
This is the path of Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنا أيضا طريقة دلالة المحدثات على الصفات، وهي أن المفعول كما دل على الفاعل، فمطلقه يدل على القدرة وكونه محكما متقنا يدل على العلم فإن كل من رأى المفعول محكما متقنا استدل بكونه مفعولا على قدرة فاعله عليه، وبكونه محكما على علم فاعله به حتى إن من زعم أن ديباجا منقشا أو دارا فاخرة فيها نقوش وتصاوير، وهي مبنية على غاية الإحكام والإتقان عاجز عنها، استجهل ونسب إما إلى الحماقة وإما إلى العناد والمكابرة. كما أن من زعم أن ذلك كله حصل بنفسه من غير ناسج ولا بأن نسب إلى ذلك. ثم بعد هذه المقدمة كانت المعتزلة بين أمور ثلاثة: فإما أن يسووا بين الشاهد والغائب في كل ذلك، ويقولون بأن قدرة الصانع ثابتة بدلالة المفعول، وعلمه ثابت بدلالة إحكام المفعول وإتقانه، كما أن ذاته ثابت بدلالة المفعولات عليه كما في الشاهد، وفي هذا ترك مذهبهم، لكنه انقياد للحق وإذعان للدليل، واعتراف بثبوت المدلول عند قيام دليل ثبوته، والتسوية بين الغائب والشاهد عند وجود التسوية. وإما أن يفرقوا بين الشاهد والغائب في كل ذلك ويقولوا بأن المفعول وإن دل على وجود الفاعل وقدرته وإحكامه وإن دل على علمه في الشاهد، ففي الغائب لا يدل على شيء، لا على وجود الفاعل ولا على قدرته وعلمه فينكرون الصانع وقدرته وعلمه، فيلزمهم إنكار الصانع، وإن وجد دليل وجوده، كما أنكروا قدرته وعلمه، وإن وجد دليل وجودهما وإما أن يسووا بين الشاهد والغائب في حق دلالة المفعول على الفاعل، ويقروا بثبوت الصانع، ويفرقوا بين الشاهد والغائب في حق دلالته على القدرة والعلم ويقولوا : وإن كان ذلك دليلا عليهما في الشاهد وليس بدليل عليهما في الغائب فيصيروا مناقضين من وجهين: أحدهما بالتفرقة بين الدلالة على الذات والدلالة على الصفات والآخر بالتفرقة بين الشاهد والغائب في الدلالة على الصفات ومتعلقنا في ذلك الاستدلال بالشاهد ووجود التسوية بينه وبين الغائب والاستدلال بالانقياد للدلالة على الذات والقول به والله الموفق.
We also have the method of the indication of originated things for the attributes, which is that the effected thing, as it indicates the agent, its absolute indicates power, and its being sound and perfected indicates knowledge. For everyone who sees an effected thing sound and perfected infers by its being effected the power of its agent over it, and by its being sound the knowledge of its agent of it. Indeed, whoever claims that an embroidered brocade or a fine house containing engravings and images, built with utmost soundness and perfection, is incapable of it, is deemed ignorant and attributed either to foolishness or to obstinacy and stubbornness. Similarly, whoever claims that all that came about by itself without a weaver or a builder is attributed to that. Then after this premise, the Mu'tazilites are between three matters: Either they equate between the witnessed and the unseen in all of that, and say that the power of the Maker is established by the indication of the effected thing, and His knowledge is established by the indication of the soundness and perfection of the effected thing, just as His essence is established by the indication of the effected things upon Him as in the witnessed. In this is a departure from their school, but it is submission to truth, acquiescence to evidence, and acknowledgment of the existence of the indicated when evidence for its existence is established, and equating between the unseen and the witnessed when there is equivalence. Or they differentiate between the witnessed and the unseen in all of that and say that the effected thing, even if it indicates the existence of the agent and His power and His soundness, and even if it indicates His knowledge in the witnessed, in the unseen it indicates nothing—neither the existence of the agent nor His power and knowledge—so they deny the Maker and His power and knowledge. Thus they are compelled to deny the Maker, even if evidence for His existence exists, just as they denied His power and knowledge even if evidence for their existence exists. Or they equate between the witnessed and the unseen in respect of the indication of the effected thing for the agent, and affirm the existence of the Maker, but differentiate between the witnessed and the unseen in respect of its indication for power and knowledge, and say: even if that is evidence for them in the witnessed, it is not evidence for them in the unseen. Thus they become contradictory from two aspects: one is the differentiation between the indication for the essence and the indication for the attributes, and the other is the differentiation between the witnessed and the unseen in the indication for the attributes. Our reliance in that is reasoning by the witnessed and existence of equivalence between it and the unseen, and reasoning by submission to the indication for the essence and affirming it. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalīl) for the Creator's attributes of power and knowledge from the perfection and soundness of creation. The author first establishes the principle: just as an effect (mafʿūl) indicates an agent (fāʿil), the absolute effect indicates power, and its being sound and perfect (muḥkam mutqan) indicates knowledge. He illustrates with everyday examples: if someone sees an elaborately decorated cloth or a fine house with engravings, and then claims that the maker was incapable of such work, that person is deemed ignorant or obstinate. Similarly, one who claims such craftsmanship came about spontaneously without a weaver or builder is considered foolish. After establishing this premise, the author presents a trilemma for the Muʿtazilites (al-Muʿtazila), who deny eternal attributes. Option one: they fully analogize (taswiya) between the witnessed (al-shāhid) and the unseen (al-ghāʾib), accepting that the effect indicates the agent's power and knowledge as it indicates His existence. This would lead them to affirm the attributes, which contradicts their school, but is submission to truth. Option two: they completely differentiate (tafrīq) between the witnessed and unseen, rejecting that the effect indicates anything about the agent in the unseen. This forces them to deny the existence of the Maker, making them atheists. Option three: they make a partial analogy, accepting that the effect indicates the agent's existence but denying its indication for power and knowledge in the unseen. This creates contradiction on two counts: differentiating between the indication for the essence and for the attributes, and differentiating between the witnessed and unseen in the indication for attributes. The author concludes that the correct approach is to reason from the witnessed when there is equivalence, and to submit to the indication for the essence and affirm it.

Key Terms
المفعول
الفاعل
الإحكام
الإتقان
الشاهد
الغائب
القدرة
العلم
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from perfection only applies to this world (witnessed) and cannot be extended to the unseen (ghaib).
Correction: The author argues that if there is equivalence between the witnessed and unseen in terms of being effects, the same indication applies. Denying this leads to contradiction or atheism.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what are the three possible positions the Mu'tazilites can take regarding the analogy between the witnessed and the unseen?
A) Complete analogy, complete differentiation, or partial analogy with contradiction
B) Complete analogy, partial analogy, or denial of the Creator
C) Acceptance of all attributes, rejection of attributes, or acceptance of essence only
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The perfection of creation demands a knowledgeable Creator; any attempt to separate the effect's indication of the agent from its indication of the agent's attributes leads to contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the intricate order and perfection in your own body or in nature, do you immediately and sincerely acknowledge that this comes from a Creator with infinite knowledge, or do you sometimes let your mind attribute it to chance or natural processes? What would it take for you to truly internalize that every detail screams of a knowing Maker?
?
The Mu'tazilites accepted God's existence but denied His knowledge. Reflect: In your daily life, do you ever act as though God exists but does not see or know your inner thoughts, private sins, or hidden motives? How would your life change if you lived with the absolute certainty that His knowledge encompasses every part of you?
?
The author says that denying the indication of perfection for knowledge leads either to atheism or contradiction. Examine your own heart: Are there areas where you resist acknowledging God's attributes because they demand more of you—like accountability for your actions? Are you willing to submit fully to what the evidence requires, even if it is uncomfortable?

Al-Jubba'i on the Perfect Act as Evidence for Attributes
Al-Jubba'i states that a perfect act indicates the agent is knowing and powerful in both seen and unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's argument that a perfected act indicates the agent's knowledge and power, and how he distinguishes between the seen and unseen realms.

Core Idea
A perfect act points to a knowing, powerful agent—by attributes in the seen, by essence in the unseen.

Al-Jubba'i, a leading Basran Mu'tazilite, presents a concise argument linking a perfect act to the agent's attributes. His view makes a key distinction: in the created world, the act points to separate attributes like knowledge and power, but in God, the act points directly to His essence, since His attributes are not distinct from His essence. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Mu'tazilite position on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Jubba'i's statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that he uses the term 'al-maf'ul al-muhkam' (the perfected effect) as the starting point of inference.
2
Identify the two realms
The passage contrasts the seen (al-shahid) and the unseen (al-gha'ib). See how the inference differs in each case.
The seen refers to created objects; the unseen refers to God.
3
Analyze the inference in the seen
In the seen, the agent is knowing and powerful through separate attributes (bil-'ilm wa al-qudra). The act is evidence for those attributes.
This is the standard Mu'tazilite position that attributes are distinct from the essence.
4
Analyze the inference in the unseen
In the unseen (God), the agent is knowing and powerful by His essence (li-dhatihi). Therefore, the act is evidence for the essence, not for separate attributes.
This is the key difference from the Ash'ari/Maturidi view.
5
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: How does this argument support the Mu'tazilite denial of eternal distinct attributes? The act points to the essence, not to attributes added to it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الجبائي ومن تابعه من البصريين فإنهم يقولون: المفعول إذا كان محكما يدل على الشاهد والغائب جميعا على ما كان فاعله عالما قادرا، وفي الشاهد كان الفاعل عالما قادرا بالعلم والقدرة، فكان الفعل المحكم المتقن دليلا عليهما، وفي الغائب كان عالما قادرا لذاته، فكان ذلك دليلا على ذاته.
As for al-Jubba'i and those who follow him from the Basrans, they say: The effect (al-maf'ul), when it is perfected, indicates in both the seen and the unseen that its agent is knowing, powerful. In the seen, the agent was knowing, powerful by knowledge and power, so the perfected, precise act was evidence for them both. In the unseen, the agent is knowing, powerful by His essence, so that was evidence for His essence.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents the Basran Mu'tazilite view of al-Jubba'i. He argues that a perfect, precise act (al-fi'l al-muhkam al-mutqan) indicates that the agent is knowing (aalim) and powerful (qaadir) in both the seen and unseen realms. In the seen, the agent possesses knowledge and power as separate attributes (bil-'ilm wa al-qudra), so the act is evidence for those attributes. In the unseen (God), the agent is knowing and powerful by His essence (li-dhaatihi), so the act is evidence for His essence, implying that His attributes are not distinct from His essence.

Key Terms
الجبائي
المفعول المحكم
العالم
القادر
بالعلم والقدرة
لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Jubba'i's view is identical to the Ash'ari or Maturidi view; he differs by saying the act indicates the essence only, not separate attributes.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i distinguishes between the seen (where attributes are separate) and the unseen (where attributes are identical to the essence). In the unseen, the act indicates the essence, not separate attributes. This differs from Ash'ari and Maturidi views that affirm distinct eternal attributes.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, what does the perfected act indicate about the agent in the unseen (God)?
A) It indicates that God has knowledge and power as separate attributes.
B) It indicates that God is knowing and powerful by His essence.
C) It indicates that God is knowing but not powerful.
D) It indicates nothing about God's attributes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i argues that a perfect act points to a knowing, powerful agent: in the seen to separate attributes, in the unseen to the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that a perfect act reveals the nature of its agent—whether through separate attributes or through essence itself. But look at your own actions: the imperfect, the careless, the half-hearted. What do your daily 'imperfect acts' reveal about your knowledge and power, or lack thereof? Are you satisfied with what they reveal about your devotion to the One whose acts are perfectly precise?
?
Al-Jubba'i insists that in the unseen, God's knowing and power are not separate from His essence—He simply is Knowing, Powerful. Do you relate to Allah as a collection of attributes you invoke ('the All-Knowing','the All-Powerful') or as a single, undivided Reality who is all of that and more? When you call upon 'Ya 'Aleem, Ya Qadeer', do you feel you are calling upon separate qualities, or upon the Living Essence that owns them all?
?
If every perfected act in the universe—from the orbit of a planet to the growth of a seed—points directly to Allah's essence, then what excuse do you have for treating any act you do as disconnected from Him? Your own actions, however small, are they offered as shahid pointing to His attributes, or do they point to your own ego? Which would you rather they point to, and what will you change today to make them point to Him alone?

Al-Ka'bi's Two-Stage Inference on Attributes
Al-Ka'bi's two-stage inference on attributes from a perfect act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's two-step inference from the perfection of an act to determine whether an agent's attributes are essential or acquired.

Core Idea
A perfect act reveals agency, but not whether attributes are essential or acquired.

Al-Ka'bi, a leading Mu'tazilite, proposed that while a perfect act proves the agent is knowing and powerful, it does not automatically tell us whether these attributes are essential to the agent or acquired. A second step—assessing the possibility of opposites—is necessary. This passage presents his distinctive two-stage argument, which contrasts with other theological views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text aloud, then its translation. Notice the two conditional structures ('if...then') that al-Ka'bi uses to distinguish between a contingent agent and God.
The passage is concise but dense.
2
Identify the two stages of inference
First stage: the perfect act indicates the agent is 'knowing, powerful only.' Second stage: we ask whether ignorance/incapacity are possible or impossible. The answer determines whether attributes are acquired or essential.
This is a key theological distinction.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why al-Ka'bi insists on a second step. What does this say about his view of the relationship between acts and attributes? Consider how this differs from other schools.
This connects to broader debates in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكعبي ومن تابعه من البغداديين يقولون: الفعل المحكم المتقن يدل على أن فاعله عالم قادر فحسب، ثم ننظر بعد ذلك فإن كان الفاعل يجوز عليه الجهل والعجز علم أنه عالم بعلم قادر بقدرة، وإذا استحال عليه الجهل والعجز وجميع أضداد العلم والقدرة كان عالما بنفسه قادرا بنفسه.
And al-Ka'bi and those who follow him from the Baghdadis say: The perfected, precise act indicates that its agent is knowing, powerful only. Then after that we look: if the agent is such that ignorance and incapacity are possible for him, we know that he is knowing by knowledge, powerful by power. And if ignorance and incapacity and all opposites of knowledge and power are impossible for him, he is knowing by Himself, powerful by Himself.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents the Baghdad Mu'tazilite view of al-Ka'bi. He argues that the perfect act only indicates that the agent is knowing and powerful in a general sense (faqat). A second step is needed: we examine the agent. If ignorance and incapacity are possible for him (i.e., he is subject to change), then his knowledge and power are acquired attributes (bil-'ilm wa al-qudra). If ignorance and incapacity are impossible for him (i.e., he is God), then he is knowing and powerful by His essence (bi-nafsihi). This is a two-step inference distinguishing between contingent and necessary beings.

Key Terms
الكعبي
المفعول المحكم المتقن
عالم قادر فحسب
الجواز
الاستحالة
بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ka'bi denies that the act indicates attributes at all.
Correction: He does say the act indicates the agent is knowing and powerful, but the manner (essential vs. acquired) requires a second step.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ka'bi, what does the perfect act indicate about the agent?
A) It indicates that the agent is knowing by knowledge and powerful by power.
B) It indicates that the agent is knowing and powerful only.
C) It indicates that the agent is knowing and powerful by essence.
D) It indicates nothing about the agent's attributes.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's argument shows that the perfection of an act reveals the agent's abilities but not whether those abilities are essential or acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a perfect act in your life, do you merely acknowledge that God is powerful, or do you reflect on the fact that His power is essential and not acquired? How does this affect your reliance on Him?
?
The passage distinguishes between contingent and necessary beings. What does it mean for you that God's attributes are essential and cannot be separated from Him? Does your worship reflect that?
?
If you were to examine your own actions, do they reveal that your knowledge and power are acquired? How does that contrast with God's essence? Does that humble you?

Abu Hashim's Introduction of 'Haal' in Divine Attributes
Abu Hashim states that the act indicates the agent has a state (haal) for being knowing and powerful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Hashim introduced the concept of 'haal' (state) as a distinct mode in arguing for divine attributes.

Core Idea
A 'haal' is a mode of being, not an attribute, that explains how the agent is knowing and powerful.

Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, a leading Mu'tazilite, proposed a novel idea: the act of an agent indicates not just that the agent possesses attributes, but that the agent has a 'state' (haal) by which he is powerful and knowing. This state is a mode of being that neither exists nor non-exists, yet distinguishes the agent from what lacks these qualities. This passage presents the core of that theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic source text once, then read the literal English translation. Notice the key terms 'حالا' (state) and 'فارق' (differentiates).
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two states mentioned
Abu Hashim mentions two states: one for being powerful and one for being knowing. Locate these in the text and note how they are linked to the act.
3
Reflect on how this differs from attributes
Consider why Abu Hashim might posit a 'state' rather than an attribute. According to him, the state is not an entity but a mode of being. How does this affect the discussion of divine attributes?
This is a distinctive Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبو هاشم ومن تابعه يقولون : إن دلالة الفعل على أن الفاعل عالم قادر دلالة على أن له حالا لكونه عليها كان قادرا ، وأن له حالا لكونه عليها كان عالما، وأنه بهاتين الحالتين فارق ما ليس بعالم ولا قادر.
And Abu Hashim and those who follow him say: The indication of the act that the agent is knowing, powerful is an indication that he has a state (haal) by virtue of which he is powerful, and that he has a state by virtue of which he is knowing, and that by these two states he differentiates from what is not knowing and not powerful.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents the Bahshamiyya view of Abu Hashim. He introduces the concept of 'haal' (state), which is a mode of being that is neither existent nor non-existent. The act indicates that the agent has a state of being powerful and a state of being knowing. These states are not attributes but modes that distinguish the agent from what lacks knowledge and power. This was a distinctive position in Mu'tazilite kalam.

Key Terms
أبو هاشم
الحال
الدلالة
الفاعل
عالم
قادر
فارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the 'haal' is an attribute like power or knowledge.
Correction: According to Abu Hashim, the 'haal' is not an attribute but a mode of being that explains how the agent is powerful and knowing. It is not an entity.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim, what does the indication of the act establish about the agent?
A) That the agent has separate attributes of knowledge and power.
B) That the agent has a state (haal) of being knowing and a state of being powerful.
C) That the agent is knowing and powerful by essence without any state.
D) That the agent is not knowing or powerful.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim introduced the concept of 'haal' as a non-attribute mode that explains how an agent is knowing and powerful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the order of the universe, do you immediately acknowledge that its Maker must be knowing and powerful? Or do you become distracted by secondary causes? What would it mean for you to consistently see every act as a sign of God's states of knowledge and power?
?
Abu Hashim's haal is a mode that distinguishes the knowing and powerful from others. How do you distinguish yourself in your knowledge and power? Are you using these attributes to align with God's will, or to assert your own independence?
